==PAGE:3==
2
ABSTRACT
2
Abstract
Inﬂuenza A virus (IAV) may cause respiratory illness in both humans and animals, and although
most recover, severe illness with fatal outcomes may develop. Therefore, it is crucial to understand
the host response to IAV infection, especially the involvement of antiviral factors to help combat
future outbreaks of IAV. The thesis aimed to identify swine antiviral innate host genes involved in the
innate antiviral response upon inﬂuenza A H3N2 virus infection at day 3 and 14 post-inoculation.
Two origins of the H3N2 inﬂuenza virus were investigated and compared; a swine-adapted
(swH3N2) and a human-adapted (huH3N2) virus. Nasal mucosal, tracheal, and lung tissue was
examined at day 3 and day 14 post-inoculation of virus by measuring the differences in gene
expression using high-throughput qPCR and performing histopathological examinations of lesions
(H&E, MAC, and IAV staining). The results showed that regulation in the nasal mucosal tissue was
generally higher than that in the tracheal and lung tissue. The results also showed that the swH3N2
caused a higher immune response in pigs than that of huH3N2 as expressed by host gene regulation.
A similar histopathological outcome was observed, as swH3N2-infected pigs generally had more
severe lesions than the control and huH3N2-infected pigs. In conclusion, the origin of the H3N2
virus is of importance in regard to the host response.

3
INTRODUCTION
3
Introduction
Approximately 5 - 15% of the world’s population is affected annually by the respiratory illness
Inﬂuenza (Allen and Ross 2018), with up to 650,000 deaths each year (WHO, Global Inﬂuenza
Program 2017).
Inﬂuenza A is the most common type of inﬂuenza infection and is widely
spread among humans and animals because of its ability to develop and adapt to different hosts.
This adaption ability results in the development of different strains of which some becomes
seasonal, others die out, or lead to global pandemics. Subtypes of inﬂuenza A virus that routinely
circulate among humans are the H1N1 and H3N2 strains, which have the ability to survive each
year due to their ability to perform antigenic drift (Allen and Ross 2018). IAV strains are often
restricted to one host because the ability to infect a host relies on viral-speciﬁc surface proteins
that bind to host-speciﬁc receptors (Allen and Ross 2018). However, pigs have shown to be a
great mixing vessel for both human and swine-adapted strains of IAV, which has contributed
to the development of new strains as a result of gene swapping between IAV strains (antigenic
shift). Furthermore, intercontinental animal transport has contributed to the worldwide spread of
different strains and has caused several outbreaks of IAV during the years, including those with
fatal consequences (Christman et al. 2011). Due to the ongoing development of new IAV strains, it
remains crucial to investigate the immune response to differently adapted inﬂuenza viruses to keep
up with seasonal vaccines and novel antiviral therapeutics that help us combat future new outbreaks
of IAV.
The thesis aims to understand and investigate the host antiviral innate immune response in
pigs upon infection with a swine-adapted and a human-adapted H3N2 IAV strain. Therefore, the
following hypotheses were tested:
1. The pigs infected with swH3N2 IAV elicit a higher antiviral innate immune response than the
huH3N2-infected pigs due to swH3N2 being adapted to pigs
2. There will be a difference in host antiviral innate response between tissues
3. The pigs infected with swH3N2 IAV have more severe lesions than the control pigs, and
huH3N2-infected pigs
This master thesis investigates two different strains of IAV in pigs at day 3 and 14; a swine-adapted
strain H3N2 IAV (swH3N2), and a human-adapted strain H3N2 IAV (huH3N2), which are being
1
==PAGE:10==
3
INTRODUCTION
compared to non-infected pigs. This will be investigated through high throughput microﬂuidic
quantitative real-time polymerase chain reaction and histopathology from tissue samples 3 and 14
days post-inoculation. The study measures the gene expression levels of relevant antiviral innate
immune genes in three areas of the respiratory tract: the nasal mucosa, trachea, and lung. Tissue
from day 3 was examined to observe the expression of antiviral innate host genes upon H3N2 IAV
infection, whereas tissue from day 14 was examined to observe a decrease in antiviral innate gene
expression. Furthermore, a histopathological examination was performed to study the extent of
reaction to virus in each tissue.
The Respiratory Pathogen Inﬂuenza A Virus
Inﬂuenza is a respiratory disease caused by inﬂuenza virus, which belongs to the Orthomyxoviridae
family, consisting of seven genera:
Inﬂuenza A, Inﬂuenza B, Inﬂuenza C, Inﬂuenza D,
Thogotovirus, Isavirus, and Quaranjavirus. All seven viruses are negative-sense single-stranded
RNA viruses (Desselberger et al. 1980; Hause et al. 2014; Presti et al. 2009). Inﬂuenza A virus
(IAV) is an enveloped virus, typically has a spherically or elliptically shape, and is ⇠80 - 120
nm in diameter (Noda 2012). The viral envelope is a lipid bilayer acquired from the host cell
membrane (Shaw et al. 2008) and protects the genetic material of the virus (see section Life Cycle
of Inﬂuenza A Virus for detailed description). The virus contains eight segments that each form viral
ribonucleoproteins (vRNPs) and encodes 20 proteins (Mostafa et al. 2018). This thesis will only
focus on 12 IAV proteins, see Table 1.

Table 1: The eight viral RNA segments of inﬂuenza A virus and twelve of their proteins. The function of the
proteins is brieﬂy mentioned in the table, however, the proteins do have broader functions (Krammer et al.
2018).
The three largest viral RNA segments of Inﬂuenza A (segment 1 - 3) encode the ﬁve proteins:
Polymerase Basic Protein 2 (PB2), Polymerase Basic Protein 1 (PB1), Polymerase Basic Protein
1-F2 (PB1-F2), Polymerase Acidic Protein (PA), and Polymerase Acidic Protein-X (PAX). The
PB1, PB2, and PA proteins form a complex and act together as the viral polymerase, which is bound
to the vRNPs (see Figure 1). PB1-F2 and PA-X proteins can inhibit the host antiviral immune
system, supporting the IAV replication (Eisfeld, Neumann, and Kawaoka 2014; Krammer et al.
2018). The fourth segment encodes the hemagglutinin (HA) transmembrane protein, which primary
function is to initiate infection by binding to the host cell (Krammer et al. 2018; Veit and Thaa
2011). The ﬁfth segment encodes the nucleoprotein (NP), which is a monomer that assembles
into an oligomer. The NP and polymerase bind to the RNA to form the vRNP, and the NP gives
structural support for both the vRNPs and the polymerase (Figure 1) (Hu et al. 2017). The sixth
3
==PAGE:12==
3
INTRODUCTION
segment encodes the neuraminidase (NA) transmembrane protein, which cleaves the sialic acid
residue from glycans in the host mucus, enabling movement through the mucus. NA proteins can
also cleave sialic acid from the host receptors, which is crucial for viral release of new progeny
viruses (Gubareva and Mohan 2022; McAuley et al. 2019). The seventh segment encodes two
proteins, namely the matrix 1 (M1) protein and the matrix 2 (M2) transmembrane protein. The
M1 protein lines the viral envelope and binds to the viral membrane and the vRNPs, and therefore
plays a major role in the morphogenesis of IAV (Figure 1) (Chaimayo et al. 2017; Veit and Thaa
2011). The M2 protein is a proton channel activated by an acidic environment. The activation of
M2 is essential during the replication of IAV and is described in detail in the section Life Cycle
of Inﬂuenza A Virus (Krammer et al. 2018; Veit and Thaa 2011). The eighth segment encodes the
non-structural protein 1 (NS1) and the nuclear export protein (NEP). NS1 can inhibit multiple host
proteins during the host antiviral response (Krammer et al. 2018). The NEP protein is important for
nuclear export of the vRNPs to the cytoplasm, which ensures that the segments can be packaged
into daughter virions (Paterson and Fodor 2012).
Figure 1: Illustration of Inﬂuenza A virus, and zoom in on the viral ribonucleoprotein. Abbreviations: Matrix
Protein 1 (M1); Matrix Protein 2 (M2); Polymerase Acidic Protein (PA); Polymerase Basic Protein 1 (PB1);
Polymerase Basic Protein 2 (PB2); single-stranded RNA (ssRNA). Adapted from (Krammer et al. 2018;
Eisfeld, Neumann, and Kawaoka 2014). Created with Biorender.
Furthermore, the inﬂuenza virus can be divided into subtypes based on the HA and NA proteins.
Currently, there are 18 antigenic subtypes of HA and are named H1 – H18; the NA protein has
11 antigenic subtypes named N1 – N11. These proteins are used to name different IAVs, e.g.,
4
==PAGE:13==
3
INTRODUCTION
H3N2 (Krammer et al. 2018). Currently, only H1N1 and H3N2 are circulating in humans; however,
it is possible that other subtypes may infect humans (Centers for Disease Control and Prevention
2021).
Life Cycle of Inﬂuenza A Virus
The HA protein of inﬂuenza A virus is a homodimer and is one of the two inﬂuenza glycoproteins.
When the virus infects the host, HA binds to a sialic acid (SA) containing receptor located on the
surface of the host’s cell membrane. Inﬂuenza A virus of human-origin has highest preference for
the alpha-2,6-linked SA receptors, since these are present in the upper respiratory tract of humans.
Inﬂuenza A virus of avian-origin has highest preference for alpha-2,3-linked SA receptors since
these are present in the upper respiratory tract of birds. Lastly, inﬂuenza A virus of swine-origin
binds to both the alpha-2,6-linked and alpha-2,3-linked receptors (Skehel and Wiley 2000; Starbæk
et al. 2018). Following binding to SA residues of the receptor, the virus enters the host cell through
receptor-mediated endocytosis and is internalized in an endosome, which then fuses with lysosomes
(steps 1, 2, and 3 in Figure 2). The acidic lysosome contains enzymes (Caffrey and Lavie 2021),
which can digest foreign contents. However, inﬂuenza A virus uses the low pH to its advantage,
as it opens the inﬂuenza M2 ion channel, allowing protons to enter the virus and thereby making
the core of the virus acidic. This interrupts the protein-protein interaction between inﬂuenza’s M1
protein and its viral ribonucleoprotein (vRNP), crucial for viral replication. Thus, initiating the
uncoating process, which is the ﬁrst major step in the viral replication after cell entry. Additionally,
the low pH also causes the HA protein to perform a conformational change, resulting in fusion of
the viral and endosomal membranes. When the two membranes are fused, the viral RNA genome
can enter the host’s cytoplasm. This completes the uncoating process (step 4 in Figure 2) (Skehel
and Wiley 2000; Pinto and Lamb 2006).
The inﬂuenza virus now has to be transcribed and replicated in the host cell’s nucleus.
The
virus contains nuclear localization signals that help transport the viral RNA segments from the
cytoplasm to the host cell’s nucleus (step 5 in Figure 2) (Eisfeld, Neumann, and Kawaoka 2014).
The RNA segments that are transported into the nucleus are negative-sense RNA strands, meaning
that they are non-coding segments and have to be copied into complementary positive-sense RNA
to be used for translating proteins. To make a positive-sense RNA strand, the negative-sense strand
must steal a 5’-cap from the host messenger RNA (mRNA) and add it to its 5’ terminal end. This
5
==PAGE:14==
3
INTRODUCTION
process is called cap-snatching and involves cutting off and stealing the 5’-cap of the host mRNA
by the viral RNA polymerase. The viral RNA polymerase then uses the cap to start transcription,
resulting in a positive-sense viral mRNA strand with a poly-A tail (Dou et al. 2018) (steps 6 and
7 in Figure 2). The newly formed viral positive-sense mRNA is then exported out of the nucleus
and into the cytoplasm, where host ribosomes translate it (step 8 in Figure 2). The mRNA coding
for the internal proteins (PB2, PB1, PB1-F2, PA, NP, NS1, and NEP) and M1 are translated in the
cytoplasm, whereas those coding for the envelope proteins (HA, NA, and M2) are translated on the
endoplasmic reticulum (ER). The newly synthesized internal proteins and M1 are then translocated
to the nucleus (step 9 in Figure 2) (Dou et al. 2018), where the viral protein NP stabilizes the
transcription of positive-sense viral RNA through acetylation; and the newly formed viral RNA
polymerase (PB2, PB1, and PA) participates in the production of full-length negative-strand RNA.
The negative-strand RNA is then used for the generation of new viral genomes. The RNA genome
segments coupled with the viral RNA polymerase and the rest of the nucleus-located proteins are
then exported from the nucleus to the cytoplasm (steps 10 and 11 in Figure 2) (Matsuoka et al.
2013). Transport vesicles move the envelope proteins, made on the ER, to the Golgi apparatus,
where they are modiﬁed and packaged for transport. The newly formed RNA segments coupled
with the viral polymerase, the nucleocapsid/internal proteins, and envelope proteins are then
packaged into progeny viruses, where they bud out of the cell membrane of the host (steps 12, 13,
and 14 in Figure 2), ready to infect other host cells (Matsuoka et al. 2013; Yadav et al. 2016). The
life cycle of inﬂuenza A virus is simpliﬁed in Figure 2.
6
==PAGE:15==
3
INTRODUCTION
Figure 2: Simpliﬁed illustration of Inﬂuenza A life cycle. 1) Viral attachment on sialic acid receptor 2)
Receptor-mediated endocytosis 3) Fusion with lysosome 4) Uncoating process 5) Nuclear import of viral
RNA 6) Transcription 7) mRNA maturation 8) translation 9) Nuclear translocation of replication proteins
10) Replication, 11) Export of progeny viral ribonucleoprotein (vRNP) 12) Packaging 13) Release of progeny
virus 14) Reinfection. Adapted from (Adeola 2017). Created with Biorender.
The Zoonotic Potential of Inﬂuenza A Virus
IAV has a great potential of being zoonotic, meaning jumping from non-human animals to humans.
IAV has a high mutation rate and obtains a high genetic diversity leading to new strains that might
jump from non-human animals to humans and thereby cause global health problems. The natural
7
==PAGE:16==
3
INTRODUCTION
reservoir of IAV is aquatic birds, and therefore almost all subtypes of IAV are classiﬁed as avian
inﬂuenza viruses, however, IAV can shift to infect other animals. IAV can achieve genetic diversity
in two ways, either by antigenic drift or antigenic shift. Antigenic drift can help the IAV escape the
immune system due to the accumulation of point mutations during replication. The hemagglutinin
and neuraminidase proteins are common targets for mutations, and these mutations will make it
more complicated for the host immune system to recognize the IAV. Antigenic shift leads to new
subtypes of IAV due to genetic material being reassorted between two or more subtypes of IAV.
This is possible because the virus comprises eight separate viral segments (Krammer et al. 2018;
Mostafa et al. 2018). Pigs are interesting when discussing antigenic shift, because they are a good
’mixing vessel’ of different types of IAV. They are susceptible to inﬂuenza A viruses from both
domestic birds and humans (Figure 3), since pigs contain both the SA↵2,6-linked receptor and the
SA↵2,3-linked receptor in the respiratory tract, making them great ’mixing vessels’. However, the
transmission of IAV from wild aquatic birds to pigs and humans, and transmission from domestic
birds to humans can also occasionally happen and are shown with dotted lines in Figure 3 (Ma,
Kahn, and Richt 2008).
Figure 3: Transmission route of Inﬂuenza A Virus (IAV). IAVs are transmitted from wild aquatic birds to
domestic birds through the natural reservoir. Virus can transmit from domestic birds to pigs, which can
further transmit IAV to humans, since pigs contain both the human SA↵2,6-linked receptor and the avian
SA↵2,3-linked receptor. Therefore, pigs are great mixing vessels for reassortment due to antigenic shift. Solid
lines; frequent and/or conﬁrmed transmission events. Dotted lines; possible and/or occasional transmission
events. Adapted from (Ma, Kahn, and Richt 2008). Created with Biorender.
8
==PAGE:17==
3
INTRODUCTION
Antigenic shifts have led to several pandemics, where the virus has evolved into new strains, see
Figure 4. As the literature describes, it began with the H1N1 that caused the ﬁrst outbreak called
the Spanish ﬂu, shown in the ﬁrst box in Figure 4. It is discussed whether the human H1N1 IAV
was transmitted directly from avian to human or the result of an antigenic shift with a mammal
as an intermediary host.
During the 1950s, an avian H2N2 IAV and the human H1N1 were
circulating, resulting in an antigenic shift in an animal that resulted in a human-adapted H2N2
strain called the ’Asian inﬂuenza’. This human H2N2 strain circulated in humans until 1968, where
the human H3N2 IAV started, also called the ’Hong Kong inﬂuenza’. The human H3N2 IAV
resulted from an antigenic shift between an avian H3N2 IAV and the human H2N2 IAV (Belshe
2005), and was ﬁrst conﬁrmed in Taiwanese pigs in 1970 (Ma, Kahn, and Richt 2008). From
the 1990s, more than 87% of America’s pig export were transported to Mexico. The extensive
transport of live pigs from America into Mexico has resulted in Mexico becoming a center for IAV
diversity due to human-to-pig transmission, long-distance movements of pigs, and antigenic shifts,
which can result in new novel viruses. The latest pandemic called the A/H1N1pdm09 resulted
from multiple antigenic shift events in Mexico. There are different theories about how this strain
emerged, but several sources state that it started with a triple antigenic shift between the human
H3N2, swine H1N1, and avian H1N1 IAV, resulting in the swine H3N2 and swine H1N2, see
Figure 4. The swine H1N2 was then mixed with a Eurasian swine H1N1 resulting in the human
A/H1N1pdm09 (Christman et al. 2011). Based on the segments in the A/H1N1pdm09, it should
not be fatal to humans, but it was due to many random but consequential mutations. The mutations
happen to be in areas associated with host speciﬁcity and virulence which is how it spread to humans
with great severity (Christman et al. 2011). This paints the picture of how new strains of IAV viruses
emerge through antigenic shifts, which potentially can lead to new pandemics (Belshe 2005).
9
==PAGE:18==
3
INTRODUCTION
Figure 4: Evolution of IAV. The ﬁgure shows how H3N2 inﬂuenza A virus has emerged from several rounds
of antigenic shifts between other strains of IAV. The ﬁgure further shows how antigenic shift led to the H1N1
Pandemic in 2009. Inspired by Belshe 2005. Created with Biorender.
The Innate Immune Response - The First Line of Defense
The immune response in vertebrates consists of two parts, the innate and the adaptive immune
response (Clark and Kupper 2005). The innate immune response is the body’s ﬁrst line of defense
which is a quick response to infection without having speciﬁcity or memory (Sandt, Joost H.C.M.
Kreijtz, and Guus F. Rimmelzwaan 2012). In comparison, the adaptive immune response is a slower
and speciﬁc response to pathogens that have overcome the innate immune response (Clark and
Kupper 2005).
The ﬁrst line of defense is the physical barriers consisting of a mucus layer and mucins produced
by goblet cells and other epithelial cells. This layer protects the gastrointestinal and respiratory
tract epithelial cells from intruders such as viruses and bacteria (Johansson and Hansson 2016).
The mucins catch and remove particles to prevent damage and invasion of the underlying tissue.
There are two mucin families, the secreted mucins MUC5B and MUC5AC, and the cell-attached
mucins MUC1, MUC4, MUC16, and MUC20 form a gel layer on top of the cilia coated epithelial
cells (Ridley and Thornton 2018; Mifsud, Kuba, and Barr 2021). The mucins trap the inﬂuenza
virus by binding them to sialic acid on the surface of the mucins. If a virus wants to escape mucins,
10
==PAGE:19==
3
INTRODUCTION
they have to cut the binding with e.g., the viral NA protein. If a virus or other intruders should pass
this ﬁrst layer of defense and infect the epithelial cells, epithelial cells will start secreting soluble
proteins. These are among others surfactant proteins (known as lectins) and ↵/β-defensins and are
produced by club cells and neutrophils, respectively. The surfactant proteins interact with the virus
by preventing it from binding to the host cells, and defensins neutralize virus (Mifsud, Kuba, and
Barr 2021). The complement system is also activated, which works by coating pathogens, making
it susceptible to phagocytosis. Moreover, the innate immune cells are activated, which include
activation of macrophages, dendritic cells, granulocytes (neutrophils, eosinophils, and basophils),
mast cells, and natural killer cells. These can be divided into different functions, the phagocytes
which include macrophages, dendritic cells, and neutrophils (Marshall et al. 2018). They engulf
microorganisms, foreign substances, and apoptotic cells and kill them inside an anti-microbial
phagolysosome (Uribe-Querol and Rosales 2020). Moreover, neutrophils also have the ability to
release anti-microbial granules (Marshall et al. 2018). Another function of the innate immune
cells is that they communicate via secretion of many different cytokines and chemokines. The
cytokines are used as communication, whereas chemokines direct other immune cells to the infected
tissue (Lacy and Stow 2011). The NK cells are activated and attracted to the site of infection by
stimuli from type I interferons and interleukins, which are produced by other immune cells. They
NK cells infected cells by releasing granules containing proteins that cause lysis of target cells, such
as perforins and granzymes. The dendritic cells and partly the macrophages serve an important role
as messengers to the adaptive immune system by presenting viral antigens to the T cell, which can
then start the speciﬁc response to infection (Marshall et al. 2018).
The innate immune cells also have intracellular pathways induced by Pattern recognition
receptors (PRRs) such as retinoic acid-inducible gene I (RIG-I)-like receptor family (RIG-I),
melanoma differentiation-associated gene 5 (MDA5). The PRRs recognize pathogen-associated
molecular patterns (PAMPs) which are molecules derived from pathogens. Recognition through
these pathways starts an intracellular cascade that leads to the release of several pro-inﬂammatory
cytokines and interferons that help clear the infection (Chen et al. 2018; Iwasaki and Pillai 2014).
Antiviral Innate Immune Response Against Inﬂuenza Virus Infection
Inﬂuenza A virus enters the host through the oral and nasal cavities, where it initiates the infection
in the respiratory tract. The respiratory epithelium is protected by mucus, the ﬁrst defense the virus
needs to pass. After passing the mucus layer, the virus attaches to the respiratory epithelial cells
11
==PAGE:20==
3
INTRODUCTION
and can then spread further in the tissue. When a virus has infected a cell, it will activate the rapid
innate response, which includes recognizing PAMPs by several PRRs and proteins (Iwasaki and
Pillai 2014).
Recognition of Pathogen-Associated Molecular Patterns
PAMPs presented by virus-infected cells activate several groups of PRRs; namely Toll-like
receptors (TLR3, TLR4, and TLR7/8), retinoic acid-inducible gene I (RIG-I)-like receptor
family (RIG-I, melanoma differentiation-associated gene 5 (MDA5) and laboratory of genetics
and physiology 2 (LGP2)), the NOD-like receptors NOD-LRR- and pyrin domain-containing 3
(NLRP3 and NOD2) (Chen et al. 2018) and Z-DNA binding protein 1 (ZBP1) (Malik and Zhou
2020). There are many different TLRs, but TLR3, 4, and 7/8 are relevant in viral infection, and they
are all presented by the epithelial cells in the respiratory tract and other immune cells (McClure and
Massari 2014).
TLR3 and TLR7/8 are placed on the surface of endosomes and lysosomes and recognize
viral PAMPs, more speciﬁcally viral RNA (Chen et al. 2018). The TLRs’ architecture is built
by an N-terminal leucine-rich repeat (LRR)-containing domain, a transmembrane domain, and a
C-terminal cytoplasmic Toll/IL-1 receptor (TIR) domain. As visualized on Figure 5, the N-terminal
LRR domain binds the ssRNA, and initiates homodimerization of TLR7, followed by MyD88
recruitment by the dimerized TIR domain. TLR8 does not undergo homodimerization because it is
suggested to be a preexisting inactive homodimer. Hence it is activated when it binds ssRNA, which
results in the binding of MyD88 to the TIR domain. The binding of MyD88 initiates the binding of
IL-1R-associated kinases (IRAK) 1 and 4, which triggers IRAK1 to bind TNF receptor-associated
factor 6 (TRAF6). TRAF6 then binds to TGFβ-activated kinase 1 (TAK1), activating two pathways.
One pathway involves activating mitogen-activated protein kinases (MAPKs), which activates the
transcription factor activator protein-1 (AP-1). The other pathway involves activation of the IKK
complex, which leads to the activation of the transcription factors (NFB) and/or IFN-regulatory
factor 7 (IRF7). Thus, activation of all three transcription factors (AP1, IRF7, and NFβ) leads to
transcription of type I + III interferons and pro-inﬂammatory cytokines (Malik and Zhou 2020).
TLR3 is expressed by mDCs, macrophages, and epithelial cells and recognizes dsRNA. Further,
TLR3 has the ability to recognize unidentiﬁed RNA structures that are present in phagocytosed
12
==PAGE:21==
3
INTRODUCTION
cells infected with inﬂuenza A virus. TLR3 is also able to enhance the activity of NFβ and IRF7,
but in contrast to TLR7/8, it uses the TIR-domain-containing adapter-inducing interferon-β (TRIF)
to catalyze the reaction instead of MyD88, as shown in Figure 5. When binding TRIF, it can interact
with TRAF3 and TRAF6. TRAF3 activates the IKK complex, which further binds the TRAF
Family-Member-Associated NFKB Activator (TANK)-binding kinase 1 (TBK1) that activates the
IRF3, which induces transcription of type 1 interferons. Another cascade of signaling is started
by TRIF-TRAF6 binding. Activation of TRAF6 facilitates ubiquitination of receptor-interacting
protein kinase 1 (RIPK1), which activates TAK1 leading to the activation of the same transcription
factor as TLR7/8 activation (Malik and Zhou 2020). Thereby, TLR3 also plays a vital role in
inﬂuenza A sensing and control (Chen et al. 2018; Iwasaki and Pillai 2014).
The membrane-bound TLR4 is also relevant when discussing viral infections because it can
bind Damage-Associated Molecular Patterns (DAMPs) that are remnants of virus-infected cells.
The binding of DAMPs by TLR4 initiates a signaling cascade through MyD88 and TRIF,
which leads to the transcription of type I + III interferons and pro-inﬂammatory cytokines, see
Figure 5 (Malik and Zhou 2020). The type I and III interferons are crucial for expressing the
interferon-stimulated genes (ISGs) that are essential in inhibiting the infection and replication of
IAV (Chen et al. 2018). Type I interferons IFN-↵and IFN-β bind to the IFN-↵/β receptor (IFNAR),
which initiates a signaling cascade by acting in an autocrine or paracrine manner. By binding
to the receptor, conformational changes allow the Janus Kinase 1 (JAK1) and Tyrosine Kinase 2
(TYK2) to bind and get phosphorylated. JAK1 and TYK2 will then bind and phosphorylate Signal
Transducer and Activators of Transcription (STAT) 1 and STAT2. The two STAT proteins will
form a complex with IRF9 and translocate to the nucleus. In the nucleus the complex will bind
the Interferon-Sensitive Response Element (ISRE) situated in the promoters of ISGs, to activate
transcription of ISGs, which are important to combat viral infections (Chen et al. 2018). Type III
interferons activate the same signaling cascade as type I interferons; however, they are differently
expressed. The receptors for type I IFNs are expressed on almost all cells, whereas the receptor
for type III IFNs are expressed on epithelial cells and some immune cells. This results in different
purposes; the type I IFNs prevent systemic infection, whereas type III IFNs prevent initial local
virus replication (Wack, Terczy´nska-Dyla, and Hartmann 2015; Stanifer et al. 2020).
13
==PAGE:22==
3
INTRODUCTION
Figure 5: The downstream pathways of TLR3, 4, 7, and 8 upon activation. TLR7/8 is a toll-like receptor
placed on endosomes and lysosomes. TLR7/8 recognizes ssRNA, which starts a cascade of reactions. When
recognizing ssRNA, the receptor becomes activated, which leads to the activation of AP-1, NFβ, and/or
IRF7, which initiate the transcription of type I and III interferons and pro-inﬂammatory cytokines. TLR3 is
activated by binding dsRNA, which will activate IRF3 and induce transcription of type I interferons. The
TLR4 is activated by DAMPs leading to the production of type I + III IFN and pro-inﬂammatory cytokines.
The produced type I IFNs will be transported out of the cell and bind to IFNAR, which starts the JAK-STAT
signaling pathway and transcription of ISGs. Adapted from Malik and Zhou 2020. Created with Biorender.
Other PRRs relevant for ﬁghting virus infection are the cytosolic RIG-I receptor, MDA5 receptor,
and LGP2 receptor (Hage et al. 2022). They all detect short dsRNA in the cytosol and nucleus
of inﬂuenza-infected epithelial cells (Malik and Zhou 2020). Even though the inﬂuenza A virus
is an ssRNA virus, it has a short 5’-triphosphate dsRNA panhandle structure at the end of the
genome that can be recognized by these receptors (Li et al. 2014).
All of the receptors have
a C-terminal domain (CTD) part and a helicase domain, and RIG-I and MDA5 also have two
14
==PAGE:23==
3
INTRODUCTION
N-terminal Caspase Activation and Recruitment Domains (CARDs) (Dias Junior, Sampaio, and
Rehwinkel 2019). The CTD has a binding pocket that binds the ssRNA 5’-triphosphate, whereas
the helicase domain binds with the base-pair region of dsRNA. The RIG-I and MDA5 receptors
work in a similar manner; the following describes how RIG-I works. As shown in Figure 6, the
binding of dsRNA results in conformational changes, which allow the CARDs region to undergo
polyubiquitination by various ubiquitin ligases such as TRIM25, Riplet, TRIM4, and Mex-3 RNA
binding family member C (MEX3C). This results in RIG-I assembling into tetramers, which interact
and activate Mitochondrial Antiviral Signaling Protein (MAVS) via their CARD domains. Activated
MAVS interacts with the TRADDosome (consisting of the TNFR-associated death domain
protein (TRADD), FAS-associated protein with death domain (FADD), and Receptor-interacting
serine/threonine-protein kinase 1 (RIPK1)).
MAVS also recruits TRAF3, which ubiquitinates
RIPK1 and hence activates the IKK complex, which induces transcription of NFβ. MAVS further
interacts with TRAF Family Member-Associated NFKB Activator (TANK), which interacts with
TBK1 and IKK". The previously mentioned IKK complex also activates the bound TBK1 and
IKK", which activate IRF3 and IRF7. Activation of RIG-I ends with the transcription factors NFβ,
IRF3, and IRF7, which initiate the transcription of type I and III interferons and pro-inﬂammatory
cytokines necessary for the antiviral immune response (Malik and Zhou 2020). The LGP2 receptor
lacks the CARDs region, which can be considered as a lack of interaction or signaling domain,
and it can therefore not initiate downstream MAVS-related signaling events (Bruns et al. 2014).
However, LGP2 does serve an important role against viruses as it is upregulated during viral
infection, and many studies have shown that it both up- and down-regulates the activity of RIG-I
and MDA5 (Rehwinkel and Gack 2020; Bruns et al. 2014).
15
==PAGE:24==
3
INTRODUCTION
Figure 6: RIG-I activation. RIG-I detects the 5’-triphosphate dsRNA panhandle structure and undergoes
conformational changes, allowing ubiquitin ligases to ubiquitinate the CARD region.
RIG-I goes into
tetramers and interacts with MAVS. MAVS bind to the TRADDosome and recruits TRAF3.
TRAF3
ubiquitinates parts of the TRADDosome, activating the IKK complex, which further activates the
transcription factor NFβ. MAVS can also bind TANK, which binds TBK1 and IKK", which further activates
the transcription factors IRF7 and IRF3. IRF7, IRF3, and NFβ initiate the transcription of type I + III and
pro-inﬂammatory cytokines. Adapted from Malik and Zhou 2020. Created with Biorender.
Several other proteins are also essential for recognizing and combating viral infections, including
ZBP1, NLRP3, and NOD2. The ZBP1 is primarily in the cytosol but is also found in the nucleus
during infection. The structure of the ZBP1 is in three parts. It has the N-terminal Z-DNA binding
(Z↵1 and 2), two central RHIM domains, and a CTD. ZBP1 can bind both DNA and RNA, resulting
in different responses.
This downstream pathway will be elaborated further since inﬂuenza A
virus is an RNA virus. By binding viral RNA, the ZBP1 interacts with RIPK3 and RIPK1. The
16
==PAGE:25==
3
INTRODUCTION
interaction between the protein complexes happens through the RHIM domain on all three protein
complexes (see Figure 7). Further, a Fas-associated protein with death domain (FADD) and Caspase
8 binds to the complex, which results in apoptosis (programmed cell death). It is also possible for
the ZBP1 to interact with RIPK1 alone, which activates the transcription factor NFβ that leads
to the transcription of pro-inﬂammatory cytokines, which are essential for the antiviral response.
Another downstream pathway of ZBP1 is the binding of RIPK3, which facilitates phosphorylation
of Mixed-lineage kinase domain-like protein (MLKL) and initiates necroptosis. It is not only ZBP1
that is activated by Caspase 8; the NLRP3 inﬂammasome is also activated by Caspase 8, as described
in the next section.
From the NLR family are especially NLRP3 and NOD2 important in innate immunity against
viral infections. The NLRP3 protein belongs to one of three subgroups of NLRs based on its
nature in the N-terminal domain (G. Correa, Milutinovic, and C. Reed 2016). NLRP3 consists
of an N-terminal pyrin domain (PYD), a central nucleotide-binding and oligomerization (NOD)
domain, and a C-terminal LRR domain. As shown in Figure 7, the active form of NLRP3 is
called the NLRP3 inﬂammasome consisting of the NLRP3, Apoptosis-associated speck-like protein
containing a CARD (ASC), and caspase-1, which is initiated via two steps.
The ﬁrst step is
the priming step, where inﬂammasome components (NLRP3, caspase 1, NEK-7 pro-IL-1β, and
pro-IL18) are upregulated. This is, among others, achieved by NFβ activation by the PRRs’
recognition of PAMPs and DAMPS, RIG-I, and ZBP1 activation. The next step is the activation of
NLRP3, which happens by oligomerization of NLRP3 that afterward binds to ASC by PYD-PYD
interactions.
The ASC recruits pro-caspase 1 and bind via CARD-CARD interactions which
result in self-cleavage of pro-caspase 1 to caspase 1 that activates pro-IL-1β and pro-IL18 to
mature IL-1β and IL-18.
IL-1β and IL-18 are both pro-inﬂammatory cytokines that induce a
continued immune response.
The active caspase 1 also cleaves the pyroptotic factor GSDMD
into GSDMDNterm, which induces pyroptosis by permeabilizing the plasma membrane (cell death
associated with inﬂammation) (Figure 7) (Malik and Zhou 2020). The NOD2 protein belongs to
another N-terminal subgroup, namely the one containing a CARDs region. The NOD2 proteins are
found in the cytoplasm of macrophages and DCs, among others, and recognize peptidoglycan from
both gram-negative and gram-positive bacteria as well as ssRNA from viruses. When NOD2 binds
viral ssRNA, it activates the MAVS protein and initiates the downstream pathway similar to RIG-I,
which activates the transcription factor IRF3 and transcription of type I interferons (G. Correa,
Milutinovic, and C. Reed 2016).
17
==PAGE:26==
3
INTRODUCTION

domain. The RIPK1 binds FADD, which binds Caspase 8. Caspase 8 activation results in apoptosis or
activation of the NLRP3 inﬂammasome. ZBP1 can also bind RIPK1, which results in the activation of
transcription factor NFβ which leads to the transcription of pro-inﬂammatory cytokines. The binding of
ZBP1 and RIPK3 also facilitates phosphorylation of MLKL, which initiates necroptosis. NLRP3 activation:
The priming is facilitated by PRR, RIG-I, and ZBP1 activation and results in transcription of NLRP3
inﬂammasome components.

interactions. The ASC binds pro-caspase 1, which results in the activation of pro-caspase 1 to become
caspase 1. Caspase 1 activates pro-IL-1β and pro-IL18 to become IL-1β and IL-18, and caspase 1 also
cleaves GSDMD into GSDMDNterm, which induces proptosis. Adapted from Malik and Zhou 2020. Created
with Biorender.

The Role of Inﬂammatory Cytokines and Interferons
Activation of the above-mentioned pathways results in the expression of several inﬂammatory
cytokines and type I + III interferons.
They are produced in order to increase the vascular
permeability and recruitment of inﬂammatory cells to the infected area. There are many important
cytokines in the innate immune response, due to the scope of this thesis, some selected ones are
described in Appendix 1 - Cytokines and Chemokines. The type I and III interferons are also
important to combat a viral infection as they are a part of the ﬁrst line of defense by inducing
the expression of interferon stimulated genes (ISGs). The type I IFNs are expressed everywhere in
the body, but the type III IFNs are only produced by epithelial cells that, for example, are present
in the respiratory tract and some immune cells. The type I IFNs work by binding to IFN-↵receptor
1 (IFNAR1) and IFN-↵receptor 2 (IFNAR2), expressed on all nucleated cells. This results in
activation of the JAK-STAT pathway, which further starts the transcription of ISGs, as visualized
in Figure 5.
Type III IFNs are, on the other hand, only sensed by a limited number of cells
like epithelial cells, DCs, NK cells, and neutrophils presenting the Interferon Lambda Receptor
1 (IFNLR1). Further, the type III IFNs also bind to interleukin 10 receptor 2 (IL-10R2) which
are presented by most cell types. Though, type III IFNs compete with interleukins from the IL-10
family members about the binding site of this receptor. Like the type I IFNs, the type III IFNs also
lead to the transcription of ISGs (Stanifer et al. 2020). The two receptors do have some differences,
especially in their response. The type I IFNs have high potency, fast kinetics, and transient response,
whereas the type III IFNs have lower potency, slower kinetics, and a more lasting response. The
type I interferons also give a more inﬂammatory response because many cells present the IFNAR
receptors. Due to the location of the type III IFN receptors (on respiratory cells), the type III IFN
response serves as the ﬁrst line of defense with a less inﬂammatory response. Hence, if the epithelial
barrier breaches, the more inﬂammatory type I IFNs response is initiated (Lazear, Schoggins, and
Diamond 2019).
The Role of Interferon Stimulated Genes
The transcription of ISGs mediated by IFNs is essential, since the ISGs serve many functions that
inhibit the virus from infecting the host. There are many ISGs involved in the innate immune
response; some work as antiviral effectors by inhibiting the virus life cycle, some are negative
regulators, and some are positive regulators.
The ISG genes selected for this thesis primarily
19
==PAGE:28==
3
INTRODUCTION
concern antiviral effectors and positive regulators. The antiviral effectors are shown in Figure 8. The
Figure shows where each of the ISGs have their antiviral function in the viral life cycle (Schneider,
Chevillotte, and Rice 2014). The thesis also looks at some positive regulators of the innate immune
response, such as STAT1, OASL, ADAR, IRF7, and IRF9, which contributes to the production of
more ISGs in the RIG-I, TLR, and JAK-STAT pathways (Schneider, Chevillotte, and Rice 2014;
Choi et al. 2015).
Figure 8: Target for interferon stimulated genes in the viral life cycle. 1) Viral attachment on sialic acid
receptor 2) Receptor-mediated endocytosis 3) Fusion with lysosome 4) Uncoating process 5) Nuclear import
of viral RNA 6) Transcription 7) mRNA maturation 8) translation 9) Nuclear translocation of replication
proteins 10) Replication, 11) Export of progeny viral ribonucleoprotein (vRNP) 12) Packaging 13) Release
of progeny virus 14) Reinfection. Adapted from (Adeola 2017). Created with Biorender.

==PAGE:29==

INTRODUCTION
Inﬂuenza A Virus in the Respiratory System
Inﬂuenza A virus is known to infect the respiratory tract, but the host tropism for different strains is
not clariﬁed. Ferrets have shown to be a good model for transmission of human inﬂuenza viruses.
Additionally, a study in ferrets showed that the human-adapted IAV strains have preference for
the ↵2,6-SA receptors, which is predominantly present in the human upper respiratory tract (URT)
and the upper part of the lower respiratory tract (LRT). In contrast, the avian IAV strains have
preference for the ↵2,3-SA receptors that are present on the tracheal, bronchial club cells, and
alveolar type II pneumocytes in the lower respiratory tract (Richard et al. 2020). However, even
though the human-adapted IAVs show preferences for the receptors in the URT, the ferret study
showed that both the IAV H1N1 and IAV H3N2 had viral replication in the LRT, though with lower
titers of H3N2 (Richard et al. 2020). Understanding and predicting the target areas of IAV strains
is complicated and complex. Therefore, it is important to understand the anatomy of the respiratory
system.
Anatomy of the Respiratory System
The respiratory system is composed of the URT and the LRT. The URT comprises the nose, sinuses,
pharynx, and larynx, whereas the LRT relates to the trachea and lungs, as seen in Figure 9 (Mifsud,
Kuba, and Barr 2021). The lungs are composed of several areas among these are the bronchi,
bronchioles, and alveoli. The air is transported into the bronchioles from the bottom of the trachea,
which forms the ’bronchial tree’ (an upside-down Y shape) (Healthline, Breathtaking Lungs: Their
Function and Anatomy 2018). The bronchioles further branch out to the alveoli, where oxygen
enters the blood and carbon dioxide leaves the blood (Encyclopedia Britannica, pulmonary alveolus
2018).
The URT and trachea are lined with ciliated epithelial cells that move foreign substances over
the surface. Additionally, goblet cells are found in the epithelium of the nasal epithelium, tracheal
epithelium, bronchial epithelium, and to a lesser extent, bronchiolar epithelium (Goblet Cells,
Biology Dictionary 2020). Goblet cells are responsible for producing mucus used for trapping
particles that need to be removed (Mercer et al. 2006). Lastly, the basal cells are primarily present
in trachea and decrease in numbers down to the bronchioles. The basal cells are responsible for
cell replacement upon injury of the epithelial cells (Jonsdottir et al. 2015). The bronchioles and
alveoli are lined with Club cells that produce and secrete surfactant proteins A, B, and D and
21
==PAGE:30==
3
INTRODUCTION
several detoxifying enzymes (Mercer et al. 2006). The surfactant proteins play a signiﬁcant role
in the innate immune system as they contribute to the elimination of pathogens (Schob et al. 2013).
The pulmonary neuroendocrine cells (PNEC) function as intrapulmonary sensors that can detect
if the tissue is starved of oxygen through chemoreception. The PNEC cells have been found to
be overstimulated in people with respiratory diseases such as asthma (Noguchi, Furukawa, and
Morimoto 2021). Lastly, the type I and type II pneumocytes are present in the bottom of the lungs
in the alveoli. The type I pneumocytes covers 95% of the peripheral lung surface and are closely
positioned to the capillary beds, which is the place of gas exchange. The type II pneumocytes are
precursors of type I pneumocytes and carry other important functions such as secretion of surfactant
proteins and phagocytosing apoptotic neighbor cells (Mercer et al. 2006).

part of the URT, whereas trachea and lungs are a part of the LRT. The ﬁgure illustrates what cells that are
present in the different lung compartments. Created with Biorender.
Experimental Background
RNA Extraction
The kit used for RNA extraction is the miRNeasy Mini Kit (Qiagen, #217004), see Appendix 2 -
RNA Puriﬁcation Protocol. The kit can be used to extract RNA from tissues by using phenol and
guanidine thiocyanate lysis in combination with an RNA-binding silica-membrane for puriﬁcation.
The QIAzol lysis reagent is used to lyse the cells, followed by the addition of ethanol to force
the precipitation of nucleic acids out of the solution to secure ideal binding conditions to the
silica-membrane. Further, the QIAzol lysis reagent inhibits RNases and removes cellular DNA
22
==PAGE:31==
3
INTRODUCTION
and proteins (Qiagen, miRNeasy Mini Kit, #217004 2022). The Deoxyribonuclease (DNase) is used
to break down genomic deoxyribonucleic acid (gDNA) (Qiagen, RNase-Free DNase Set #79254
2022). The RWT, RDD, and RPE buffers in the kit are used to wash away contaminants. The RWT
buffer is a thorough washing buffer used after QIAzol Lysis Reagent. It contains Guanidine salt and
ethanol and is used to wash away carbohydrates, proteins, and fatty acids that are bound to the silica
membrane (Qiagen, RWT Buffer 2022). The RDD buffer ensures that on-column DNA is digested
and RNA remains bound to the column (Qiagen, RDD Buffer 2022). The RPE is a mild washing
buffer that removes remaining salts from earlier buffers in the kit (Qiagen, RPE Buffer 2022). The
maximum binding capacity for the RNeasy Mini spin column is 100 µg RNA and binds RNA larger
than 18 nucleotides (Qiagen, miRNeasy Mini Kit, #217004 2022).
RNA Quality
The NanoDrop Spectrophotometer (ND-ONE-W, ThermoFisher) was used to examine the RNA
quantity and purity. The quantity and purity are measured through UV spectroscopy. The RNA is
measured at 260 and 280 nm, which is the absorbance of nucleotides and proteins, respectively, and
is used to detect contamination with proteins. Following Lambert-Beers law, seen in Figure 10, a
260/280 ratio gives the purity of the RNA, and the concentration is given as well (ThermoFisher,
Quantitating RNA 2022). The RNA-ratio should be 2.0 for pure RNA (ThermoFisher, NanoDrop
Spectrophotometer 2022).
Figure 10: Lambert Beer’s Law. Inspired from (ThermoFisher, Quantitating RNA 2022)
The quality of the RNA was measured on the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer Instrument (Agilent
Technologies), which indicates how intact the RNA is.
The principle for the Agilent 2100
Bioanalyzer Instrument is on-chip gel electrophoresis to analyze the size of RNA, DNA, and
proteins (Principles of Sample Analysis on a Chip, UBC 2022). RNA integrity is measured by
an RNA integrity number (RIN) ranging from 1 - 10, with 1 being most degraded and 10 being most
intact (Schroeder et al. 2006). It is calculated by a 2100 Bioanalyzer Expert Software that takes out
individual interpretations of RNA quality. The Agilent bioanalyzer system base the RIN value on the
23
==PAGE:32==
3
INTRODUCTION
ratio between the eukaryotic 18S and 28S ribosomal subunits because it indicates the level of sample
degradation in the gel electrophoresis (Schroeder et al. 2006). After analysis, the 2100 Bioanalyzer
Expert Software will return with an electropherogram showing laser-induced ﬂuorescence detection
of the 18S and 28S subunits, where degradation will show as a less intense ﬂuorescence signal. The
28S:18S ratio should be between 1.8 and 2.0 to be considered good RNA quality, and the RIN value
should be higher than 5 for reproducibility (Fleige and Pfafﬂ2006).
Quantitative Real-Time Polymerase Chain Reaction
Quantitative Real-Time Polymerase Chain Reaction (qPCR) is a measurement of the relative
quantity of target DNA in a sample (Singh and Roy-Chowdhuri 2016). Before qPCR, the RNA
is reverse transcribed into complementary DNA (cDNA) since DNA is more stable than RNA due
to RNases in the environment, among others. This is done by using a wipeout buffer to remove
all genomic DNA, reverse transcriptase (RT) to convert RNA into cDNA, and an RT primer mix
with a mix of oligo-dT and random primers that enable cDNA synthesis from all regions of RNA
transcripts (Singh and Roy-Chowdhuri 2016). The cDNA is preampliﬁed before continuing to
qPCR to get more cDNA templates (Andersson et al. 2015) using TagMan PreAmp Master Mix.
Gene-speciﬁc primers can be designed and used in qPCR to look at speciﬁc genes and/or areas of
the template DNA. The qPCR ampliﬁes and quantiﬁes PCR products in real-time by following a
predeﬁned set of cycles; denaturation, annealing, and elongation (Thermoﬁsher, Real Time qPCR
Handbook 2022). During the qPCR, the ﬂuorescence signal is measured as the amount of template
DNA that is present at the exact moment in one sample. The amount of template DNA is calculated
from the quantiﬁcation cycle (Cq), which is the cycle number where the ﬂuorescence signal crosses
the threshold (Thermoﬁsher, Real Time qPCR Handbook 2022). There is a statistically signiﬁcant
increase in signal over the calculated baseline signal (Thermoﬁsher, Real Time qPCR Handbook
2022). This means that a low Cq value equals a high concentration of mRNA. The ﬂuorescence can
be detected using a binding dye, e.g., EvaGreen or SYBR Green (Guðnason et al. 2007; Citri et al.
2012), which binds to newly synthesized DNA (Citri et al. 2012). For this experiment, EvaGreen is
used because it has less background ﬂuorescence and is less inhibitory than SYBR Green (Biotium
2022).
Primer-dimers and non-speciﬁc products can also be produced during a qPCR run. A melting
curve analysis is therefore performed to avoid any inaccurate results. Reference genes are also
included to correct for technical variations and can normalize for low RNA integrity and inhibitory
24
==PAGE:33==
3
INTRODUCTION
components in the samples.
If possible, three or more reference genes should be used for
normalization, and the reference genes should ideally not show any expression changes regardless
of conditions (Thermoﬁsher, Real Time qPCR Handbook 2022). The efﬁciency of the qPCR is
also determined by using a standard curve to correct for differences in ampliﬁcation efﬁciency.
The standard curve is based on a dilution series beginning with the highest concentrated cDNA
samples and is produced by plotting the log value of the starting quantity against the Cq value
and describes the linear relationship between the amount of starting material and the measured Cq
value (Thermoﬁsher, Real Time qPCR Handbook 2022). Lastly, it is essential to include controls to
validate data. A no-reverse transcription (-RT) control is used to test for contamination of gDNA
in the samples. Additionally, to observe if any contamination is present in the components used, a
no-template control (NTC) is added (Thermoﬁsher, Real Time qPCR Handbook 2022).
Microscopical Analysis of Tissue
Histopathology is used to examine tissue and cells through microscopy. A thin slice of tissue is
examined through an optical light microscope (Leica DMLB /100 S microscope, Leica, Germany),
where the tissue is treated with different staining methods to highlight speciﬁc cells. The most
common staining method is H&E staining (hematoxylin and eosin).
It visualizes normal and
abnormal cell structures. The hematoxylin binds to acidic structures, such as DNA and RNA, and
colors them blue, whereas the eosin colors cytoplasm, intracellular and extracellular proteins a light
pink color (Kleczek, Jaworek-Korjakowska, and Gorgon 2020).
The
MAC387
staining
method
utilizes
the
MAC387
antibody
that
binds
the
MRP8/MRP14
heterocomplex,
also
called
calprotectin,
produced
by
inﬁltrating
monocytes/macrophages/neutrophils during acute inﬂammation and macrophages found in
chronic inﬂammation (Soulas et al. 2011).
Additionally, macrophages can be challenging to
distinguish from epithelial cells, therefore, the MAC387 staining can be advantageous. Further
immunostaining for IAV can be used to detect IAV-positive cells. Staining for IAV-positive cells
depends on speciﬁc antibodies that bind the IAV nucleoprotein (NP), highlighting IAV-positive
cells. The antibody used for this thesis is the mouse monoclonal antibody HYB 340-05 produced
by Statens Serum Institut (SSI) (Statens Serum Institut, Product Speciﬁcation, HYB 340-05 2020).

Materials & Methods
Experimental Setup
Prior to this study, 30 cross-bred Landrace x Yorkshire x Duroc pigs were randomly assigned to
one of three groups. Six pigs were assigned to the control group (group 1), 12 pigs were infected
with a swine-adapted H3N2 inﬂuenza A virus (group 2), and lastly, 12 pigs were infected with a
human-adapted H3N2 inﬂuenza A virus (group 3). The inoculum was produced by Statens Serum
Institut (SSI) and contained a viral titer of 1.11 · 106 TCID50/mL. Group 1 was inoculated with
control medium, and Group 2 and 3 were inoculated with A/SW/DK/102335-1/2013 (H3N2) and
A/DK/304/2020 (H3N2), respectively (Table 2). The two strains are referred to as swH3N2 and
huH3N2, respectively, in this thesis.

Table 2: Group distribution and their inoculum
Three days after inoculation, six pigs from the control group and eight pigs from each of group 2
and 3 were euthanized. Fourteen days after inoculation, the remaining eight pigs were euthanized
(Table 2 and Figure 11). Nasal mucosal, tracheal, and lung tissue was collected for further analysis
using microﬂuidic high-throughput quantitative-PCR (qPCR) and histopathology.

inoculated with either a control medium or H3N2 Inﬂuenza A virus. The pigs were either euthanized on

Microﬂuidic High-Throughput qPCR
Microﬂuidic qPCR was used as a high-throughput measurement of genes involved in the antiviral
immune response.
Three 96x96 microﬂuidic chips were used, one for each tissue (nasal
mucosal, tracheal, and lung tissue). Seventy-nine host genes (Appendix 3 - Selected Genes with
Abbreviations) and two viral genes were selected based on a preliminary literature study. Eight of
the selected 79 host genes and one out of the two selected viral genes required design of a new primer
assay since they had not previously been validated in house. In order to perform Microﬂuidic qPCR,
the tissue was homogenized, and the RNA was ﬁrst extracted and puriﬁed before being converted
into cDNA and preampliﬁed. The workﬂow is illustrated in Figure 12.
27
==PAGE:36==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
Figure 12: Illustration of the workﬂow of the microﬂuidic high-throughput qPCR study. RNA was puriﬁed and
extracted from trachea, lung, and nasal mucosal tissue from 30 pigs. The extracted RNA is then converted into
cDNA. Following cDNA synthesis, pre-ampliﬁcation was performed prior to qPCR. 96x96 dynamic arrays
were used to test 81 genes and 90 samples.
Design of Host Gene Primers
The coding sequence of the genes that required new primer assays was found in Ensembl (Howe
et al. 2020), and the primers were designed using Primer3 (v. 0.4.0) (Koressaar and Remm 2007).
The product size range was set to be 70 - 100 nucleotides, and the melting temperature (TM) of the
primer was minimum 59◦C, optimum 60◦C, and maximum 61◦C. For thermodynamic parameters
Breslauer et al., 1986 were used (Breslauer et al. 1986). If achievable, one of the newly designed
primers would overlap an exon junction to avoid gDNA ampliﬁcation. Furthermore, to ensure that
the primer pairs were speciﬁc to the gene of interest, the basic local alignment search tool (BLAST)
software provided by National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) was used (NCBI -
BLAST 2022). Sigma-Aldrich provided the primers in a 100 µM primer solution. The solution
was then diluted in Tris-ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid (TE) buffer to 20 µM containing both the
forward and reverse primer.
RNA Puriﬁcation
The nasal mucosal, tracheal, and lung tissues were stored in -20◦C in RNAlater Stabilization
Solution (ThermoFisher Scientiﬁc, #AM7021). RNA puriﬁcation was performed according to the
protocol in Appendix 2 - RNA Puriﬁcation Protocol using the miRNeasy Mini kit (Qiagen, #217004)
28
==PAGE:37==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
and RNase-Free DNase set (Qiagen, #79254). Each sample was carved into ⇠3x3 mm. at room
temperature and transferred to an M tube containing 1 mL of QIAzol Lysis Reagent. The samples
were homogenized with a GentleMACSTM Dissociator at program RNA_02.01 and then placed at
room temperature for 5 minutes. Afterward, 200 µL chloroform was added, and the tube was shaken
vigorously for 15 seconds. The samples were then placed at room temperature for 2 - 3 minutes.
Subsequently, the samples were transferred to 1.5 µL Eppendorf tubes and centrifuged at 14000 rpm
for 15 minutes at 4◦C. This will divide the samples into three phases. 300 µL from the top phase of
each sample was moved to a 2 mL Eppendorf tube, and 450 µL 100% ethanol was added and mixed.
The next step was performed twice: 600 µL of the sample was transferred to an RNeasy Mini Spin
Column (Qiagen) in a 1.5 mL collection tube. The samples were centrifuged at 10000 rpm for 15
seconds at room temperature, and the ﬂow-through was discarded. 350 µL RWT buffer (Qiagen)
was added to the column and centrifuged at 10000 rpm for 15 seconds at room temperature, and the
ﬂow-through was discarded.
In another Eppendorf tube, a DNase Incubation Mix was mixed. Here 10 µL DNase I stock
solution (Qiagen) was mixed with 70 µL RDD buffer (Qiagen). After mixing, 77 µL of DNase I
mixture was added to the column, and the samples were placed at room temperature for 15 minutes.
350µL RWT buffer (Qiagen) was then added to the column and centrifuged at 10000 rpm for 15
seconds at room temperature; the ﬂow-through was discarded. The next step was performed twice:
500 µL RPE buffer was added to the column, centrifuged at 10000 rpm for 15 seconds at room
temperature, and the ﬂow-through was discarded. The column was then moved to a new 2 mL
collection tube and centrifuged at maximum speed for two minutes to dry the membrane of the
column. The column was then moved to a new 1.5 mL collection tube, and 50 µL RNase free water
was transferred to the membrane to elute the extracted total RNA. The samples were centrifuged
at 10000 rpm for 1 minute at room temperature. The ﬂow-through was reused and placed at the
membrane again. The samples were then centrifuged one last time at 10000 rpm for 1 minute at
room temperature. The puriﬁed RNA was then measured using a NanoDrop and a Bioanalyzer to
evaluate RNA quality and quantity.
Quality and Quantity Assessment of Samples
The purity and quantity of the extracted RNA were measured using a NanoDropTM One/OneC
Microvolume UV-Vis Spectrophotometer (Thermo Fischer Scientiﬁc). The spectrophotometer of
the NanoDrop was cleaned with 1.3 µL RNase free water and wiped down before loading of samples.
29
==PAGE:38==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
1.3 µL of the samples were then loaded on the spectrophotometer. The samples were measured at
UV absorption values at 260, 230, and 280 nanometers (nm). The result of the Nanodrop was
examined based on the amount of RNA and the purity. To continue to cDNA synthesis 300-500
µg/µL RNA was required and a 260/280 (Nucleic acid/protein absorbance) ratio 2.0 was accepted
as pure RNA. The quality of the RNA was determined by Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent
Technologies) according to the protocol in Appendix 4 - Measurement of RNA Integrity Protocol.
Quantities with RIN ≥5 were considered acceptable. The quality and quantity assessment for all
samples can be seen in Appendix 5 - Quality and Quantity Measurements
cDNA Synthesis and Preampliﬁcation
After the extracted RNA was conﬁrmed to have high quality and quantity, the RNA was synthesized
into cDNA by reverse transcription, following the protocol in Appendix 6 - cDNA Synthesis
Protocol. The QuantiTect Reverse Transcription Kit (Qiagen #205311) was used to perform the
cDNA synthesis. First, 300 ng RNA, 1.5 µL 7x gDNA Wipeout Buffer, and 11.5 µL RNase-free
water (a total volume of 14 µL) were mixed. Furthermore, duplicates of all samples were produced
to account for potential technical variations.
The samples were placed in the thermocycler
(Biometra TRIO 48) for 2 minutes at 42◦C followed by cooling at 4◦C. The gDNA wipeout buffer
has ensured that all genomic DNA is removed. A master mix containing Quantiscript reverse
transcriptase (RT), RT primer mix, 5x RT buffer, and RNase free water was mixed. 6 µL of master
mix was added to each sample except the negative controls without reverse transcriptase (-RT).
A similar master mix was added to the -RT samples, except it did not contain the enzyme reverse
transcriptase.
After the master mix had been added, it was placed in the thermocycler for 15
minutes at 42◦C ! 3 minutes at 95◦C ! 4◦C for 1. The cDNA samples were then diluted 1:10 in
1x Low EDTA TE-Buffer (pH = 8) to prepare for pre-ampliﬁcation.
Preampliﬁcation was performed following the protocol in Appendix 7 - mRNA pre-ampliﬁcation
Protocol. In order to pre-amplify all samples, a primer mix was prepared. The primer mix used
contained 5 µL of each 20 µM primer solution and TE buffer to a total volume of 500 µL. 2.5
µL of the primer mix was added to 3 µL TaqMan PreAmp Master Mix (Applied Biosystems, PN
4391128), 2 µL TE buffer, and 2.5 µL cDNA. The samples were placed in the thermocycler at 90◦C
for 10 minutes, and subsequently, 19 cycles of 95◦C for 15 seconds ! 60◦C for 4 minutes. After
the 19 cycles, the samples were placed at 4◦C for 1. The number of cycles was determined based
30
==PAGE:39==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
on results from previous projects (data not shown). The samples were treated with Exonuclease I
(New England Biolabs, PN MO293L) to remove excess primers. The exonuclease was diluted to
4U (3.5 µL MiliQ, 0.5 µL reaction buffer, 1 µL Exo 20U). 4 µL exonuclease I 4U was added to each
sample, and the samples were placed in the thermocycler at 37◦C for 30 minutes ! 80◦C for 15
minutes ! 4◦C) for 1. After incubation, the treated samples were diluted 1:10 (2 µL preampliﬁed
exonuclease treated cDNA to 18 µL 1x low EDTA TE buffer (pH = 8) and stored at -20◦C) prior to
qPCR.
Microﬂuidic High-Throughput qPCR
A 96.96 Dynamic ArrayTM IFC for Gene Expression (Fluidigm®) was used for this study, and the
protocol in Appendix 8 - qPCR Protocol was followed. Three plates were used, where each plate
contained inlets for 96 primer pairs and 96 samples (including sample replicates, dilution series,
interplate calibrators, and negative controls). An assay master mix was produced by adding 3 µL
2X Assay Loading Reagent (Fluidigm®) and 3 µL 20µM into each well of a microtiter plate. The
microtiter plate was sealed with ﬁlm and stored at 4◦C. A sample master mix was also produced
by adding 4.6 µL of a pre-sample mix (consisting of 2X TaqMan Gene Expression Master Mix
(Applied Biosystems, PN 4369016), 20X DNA binding Dye (Fluidigm), EvaGreen 20X (VWR,
BTIU31000), and 1X low EDTA TE-Buffer) into another microtiter plate, together with 1.5 µL
pre-ampliﬁed cDNA. The plate was sealed with ﬁlm and stored at 4◦C. Before using the chips, they
were inspected with Control Line Fluid (Krytox) (Fluidigm®), which is important for the chip to
work. The Control Line Fluid was added at the top and bottom valve using a syringe. The chips
were then primed in the HX IFC (Fluidigm®) controller according to a predeﬁned program (Chip
Prime (136x)). Before continuing, the assay mix and sample mix microtiter plates were vortexed
and centrifuged for 60 sec at room temperature and 1000 rpm. Each chip was then loaded with 5
µL assay master mix to the left side of the chip (the assay inlets) and 5 µL sample mix to the right
side of the chip (the sample inlets). The chip was inspected for air bubbles, although small loose
bubbles were accepted. The chip was then placed in the HX IFC (Fluidigm®) controller and was
run at the Load Mix (136x) program. Before moving the chip to the BioMark HD (Fluidigm®), any
dust was removed with tape from the integrated ﬂuidic circuit. Following dust removal, the chip
was moved to the BioMark HD (Fluidigm®), and the high-throughput qPCR was performed using
the program illustrated in Figure 13. The program is divided into different phases. The hot start
ensures DNA polymerase activation. The PCR cycle consisted of a denaturation step at 95◦C for
31
==PAGE:40==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
15 seconds for denaturation of the DNA double strands, followed by annealing and elongation at
60◦C) for 1 minute. Lastly, the melting curve analysis was conducted in the melting phase.
Figure 13: Illustration of the program used for microﬂuidic high-throughput qPCR. The x-axis is time in
seconds, and the y-axis is temperature in Celcius. The ﬁgure is obtained from Fluidigm Real-Time PCR
analysis software (v. 4.8.1)
Processing of qPCR Data
Fluidigm Real-Time PCR Analysis Software (v. 4.8.1) was used to evaluate the data visually.
To ensure speciﬁc products, the samples were evaluated based on their melting curve and their
ampliﬁcation curve. To validate the data further, the negative controls were examined. The negative
controls were either samples not containing Reverse Transcriptase (-RT) or non-template controls
(NTC). The -RT samples were compared to their respective samples. The NTCs were also evaluated
to ensure that there was no contamination. The efﬁciency of the individual qPCR assays was
corrected based on standard curves. Efﬁciency between 80% and 120% and an R2 value, calculated
from the line of best ﬁt, greater than 0.95 were accepted.
After data pre-processing in Fluidigm Real-Time PCR Analysis Software, the data were
exported for subsequent data analysis in GenExTM (MultiD). The data were analyzed in two ways;
one uses intraplate calibration only using data from one 96.96 dynamic array, and one uses interplate
calibration using data from all three 96.96 dynamic arrays. This was done in order to use one set of
data for analyzing the tissue individually and one set of data for comparing the tissues in between
32
==PAGE:41==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
by accounting for potential technical variation between plates. The host genes data was normalized
using ﬁve reference genes (GAPDH, HPRT1, RPL13A, PPIA, and YWHAZ), which had all been
validated in previous projects (data not shown). The data was normalized by using geNORM and
NormFinder (Vandesompele et al. 2002; Andersen, Jensen, and Ørntoft 2004). Samples with a
difference greater than 1.5 in Cq-value in more than 15% of the cDNA replicates were removed.
cDNA replicates were averaged, and samples with 20% or more missing values were removed. The
remaining missing data were assigned a Cq-value of +1 from the highest Cq-value of the speciﬁc
gene. The Cq-values were converted into relative quantities (RQ) to compare fold change between
groups. The data was furthermore converted into log2-scale for statistical analysis.
Data Analysis
A statistically signiﬁcant difference in expression between groups was determined by a two-tailed
t-test of the log-transformed data. The standard error of mean (SEM) and standard deviation (SD)
were also calculated. The differences in gene expression were considered statistically signiﬁcant
if p-values 0.05. Further, a principal component analysis (PCA) was performed to reduce the
dimensionality of the data by reducing the number of variables. This analysis visualizes clustered
samples by illustrating the data in a three-dimensional scatter-plot with the principal components
on the axes; PC1, PC2, and PC3. The largest possible variance on PC1, the second-largest possible
variance on PC2, and the third-largest possible variance on PC3 (Builtin, Principle Component
Analysis 2021). For this thesis, two PCAs were conducted to compare and visualize the expression
proﬁles of all samples. The ﬁrst PCA describes the expression proﬁle of the samples taken from
day 3 from all tissues, whereas the second PCA describes the expression proﬁle of the samples
taken from day 14 from all the tissue. The PCAs were conducted in order to determine if any of the
tissues showed clustering, which indicates a similar expression proﬁle of the samples taken from the
respective tissues. Additionally, if a gene was analyzed twice by the same or different primer pairs,
a correlation analysis was performed to ensure the validity of the primers. Lastly, the genes were
considered up-regulated if the gene expression was doubled compared to the control and considered
down-regulated if the gene expression was halved compared to the control.
Histopathological Preparation
Prior to this project, the lung tissue samples were all prepared with semi perfusion at necropsy
by injecting 10% neutral formalin buffer into the bronchi. All tissue samples were placed in 10%
33
==PAGE:42==
4
MATERIALS & METHODS
neutral formalin buffer for a week, dehydrated in ethanol, embedded in parafﬁn wax, and sliced
into 2 - 3 µm sections. Four samples from each group were chosen as subject for staining based on
the amount of virus detected by RT-qPCR prior to the start of this thesis. This amount of virus
detected in each sample is shown in Appendix 9 - Viral Titers in Samples. The chosen tissue
samples were stained with H&E staining to visualize both healthy and affected tissues. Further,
two additional staining methods were applied for selected samples. The chosen staining methods
were immunohistochemical MAC387 staining and IAV staining. The staining was performed by
the Laboratory of the Veterinary Pathology Group, section of Pathological Sciences, University of
Copenhagen. The protocols used for staining can be found in Appendix 10 - H&E Staining Protocol,
Appendix 11 - IAV Staining Protocol, and Appendix 12 - MAC staining Protocol.
Scoring of Stained Tissue
The scoring criteria made for each staining method and tissue were based on what was observed
in the microscope because there was no general way of scoring. The criteria were created with
supervision from Benjamin Meyer Jørgensen, research assistant at University of Copenhagen, to
ensure that we were looking after the right things in each tissue. It is essential to note that the
highest possible score in the different categories is not equal to the worst possible histopathological
outcome but is based on what was seen in the different sections. To ensure that the scoring was
not biased, the scoring was done in several rounds. First, each sample was scored by us two times;
second, randomly selected samples were scored without knowing the sample group; third, randomly
selected samples were scored by a third party. The scoring criteria were as follows:
Nasal Mucosal Tissue
– H&E staining
– Number of neutrophiles in lamina epithelialis cell layer.
– MAC387 staining
– Number of mononeuclear cells in a representative area in a 40X zoom in the lamina
propia – tela submucosa.
– IAV staining
– Not scored. No IAV positive cells were found in the nasal mucosal.
Trachea Tissue
– H&E staining

MATERIALS & METHODS
– Not scored as all sample groups looked the same
– MAC387 staining
– Number of stained cells in a representative area in a 40X zoom in the lamina propia –
tela submucosa.
– IAV staining
– Number of IAV-positive cells in the lamina propia – tela submucosa.
Lung Tissue
– H&E staining
– Inﬁltration of cells between the alveoli
– Inﬁltration of cells inside the lumen of the alveoli
– Inﬁltration of cells inside the bronchioles
– MAC387 staining
– Number of mononeuclear cells in a representative area in a 40X zoom in alveolar tissue.
– IAV staining
No criteria as only two IAV-positive cells where found in one samples.

5
RESULTS
5
Results
Quantitative PCR Results
In order to evaluate the qPCR results of the selected 79 genes, the genes were grouped based on
innate immune response mechanisms, and those that appeared interesting were visualized on graphs
in the following sections. Refer to Appendix 13 - qPCR data, Intraplate and Appendix 14 - qPCR
data - Interplate Calibration for genes not included in the graphs.
The overall picture of the qPCR data shows more regulation in the pigs infected with swH3N2
than those infected with huH3N2. This was expected due to the swH3N2 virus being adapted to
pigs and huH3N2 being adapted to humans. The qPCR data further show a trend where many
up-regulated genes on day 3 become down-regulated on day 14, which indicates that an equilibrium
is desired.
The following will present the qPCR data, which have been grouped according to
theoretical relevance and shown in bar charts with the genes on the x-axis and the relative quantity
on the y-axis.
RIG-I Related Genes are Central Against Swine-Adapted H3N2 IAV
Genes related to the RIG-I pathway for each tissue are visualized in Figure 14. RIG-I is signiﬁcantly
up-regulated in the nasal mucosal, tracheal, and lung tissue infected with the swH3N2 on day 3.
Moreover, the tracheal and lung tissue also showed some up-regulation on day 14. Further, DDX60,
which assists in the RIG-I activation, is signiﬁcantly up-regulated in all three tissues infected with
the swH3N2 on day 3. DDX60 is further also signiﬁcantly up-regulated in the tracheal tissue on
day 14. The MDA5 gene, coding for the MDA5 protein that senses viral presence similar to RIG-I,
is also up-regulated on day 3 in all three tissues infected with the swH3N2 strain. When looking at
the huH3N2-infected pigs, only the MAVS gene in the nasal mucosa on day 3 and 14 and in the
lung tissue on day 14 show signiﬁcant regulation. This indicates that the regulation of genes related
to the RIG-I pathway is mainly affected by the swH3N2 strain at the time of sampling.

H3N2 Induces ZBP1 Expression in All Tissues
Genes related to the TLR pathways, ZBP1 protein pathway, and NLRP3 pathway are visualized
in Figure 15. In general, many genes are regulated in the nasal mucosal tissue with regulation in

is signiﬁcantly up-regulated in all three tissues. In the nasal mucosal tissue, ZBP1 is signiﬁcantly

and lung tissue, ZBP1 is only signiﬁcant up-regulated in the swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3. The

on day 3. The TRAF6 gene is up-regulated in the nasal mucosal tissue for the swH3N2-infected
pigs on day 14 and the huH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 and day 14.

Antiviral Genes are Notably Regulated in the Nasal Mucosa
Some of the analyzed genes have been grouped into an antiviral category because they show antiviral
activity against IAV. From the relative quantities in expression, it is clear that more genes are
regulated in the nasal mucosal tissue than in the tracheal and lung tissue. Both the TMPRSS2
gene and ZC3HAV1 gene show signiﬁcantly up-regulation in the swH3N2 and huH3N2-infected
pigs on day 3 and 14 in the nasal mucosa.
TMPRSS2 further shows down-regulation in the
swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 in the tracheal tissue and the huH3N2-infected pigs on day 14
in the lung tissue. The ETV7 gene shows signiﬁcantly up-regulation in the swH3N2-infected pigs
on day 3 and 14 and in the huH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 in the nasal mucosa. ETV7 is also
signiﬁcantly up-regulated in the tracheal tissue for the swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3, though this
data has not been included in the graph but can be seen in Appendix 13 - qPCR data, Intraplate. Two
different primer pairs for this gene have been used, and due to the primer pairs showing correlation,
it is reasonable to say that the gene is up-regulated in the tracheal tissue. Further, ETV7 shows
signiﬁcant up-regulation in the swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 in the lung tissue. Lastly, SAMHD1
only shows signiﬁcant up-regulation in the huH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 in the nasal mucosa.

Comparison of Expression Between Tissue
Principle Component Analysis Indicate H3N2 as an Upper Respiratory Disease
The gene expression proﬁles in the different samples were examined through a principal component
analysis (PCA). Two PCAs were conducted based on 74 genes. The ﬁrst PCA (Figure 18) illustrates
all infected tissue collected on day 3. The red indicates the nasal mucosal tissue, the purple indicates
the trachea, and the blue color indicates the lung. There are three clusters and are divided based on
the tissue. The nasal mucosal and tracheal tissue is more mixed than the lung cluster, meaning that
their expression proﬁles are more similar to each other than to the lung.
Figure 18: Principle Component Analysis of the different tissue on day 3. Each tissue is deﬁned by its own
cluster. Red = nasal mucosal, purple = trachea, and blue = lung. X-axis: PC1, Y-axis: PC2, Z-axis: PC3.
The second PCA (Figure 19) illustrates all infected tissue collected on day 14. This illustrates a
different picture than those pigs that were collected on day 3. There are also three clusters in this
PCA; however, they are more distinct. The nasal mucosal and tracheal clusters are more divided on
day 14 compared to day 3. Thus, there is a different gene expression in the different tissue.
44
==PAGE:53==
5
RESULTS
Figure 19: Principle Component Analysis of the different tissue on day 14. Each tissue is deﬁned by its own
cluster. Red = nasal mucosal, purple = trachea, and blue = lung.X-axis: PC1, Y-axis: PC2, Z-axis: PC3.
Highest Gene Regulation Observed in the Nasal Mucosa
Table 3 determine which tissue elicit the highest response inside each strain for each day. From
Table 3, it can be seen that the swH3N2-infected pigs elicit the highest response in the nasal mucosa
on day 3, followed by the lung, whereas the trachea elicits the lowest immune response. When
looking at day 14, it is still the nasal mucosa that elicits the highest response, followed by the
trachea and lung that elicit a similar amount of response. On the other hand, the huH3N2-infected
pigs only elicits a noticeable response in the nasal mucosa on both day 3 and 14. This might indicate
that the huH3N2 virus has been cleared in the nasal mucosa and therefore has not been able to infect
the host further down in the respiratory tract. However, it should be considered that this study is
limited to 96 genes, and therefore there are a lot of genes involved in viral infection that are not
taken into account. If all genes involved were analyzed, the ﬁnal picture might be different, but this
gives an indication.
