==PAGE:70==
6
DISCUSSION
6
Discussion
Model Design & Lack of Expression
The aim of this study was to evaluate the innate immune response in regards to infection with
two different IAV strains based on samples taken on day 3 and day 14 post-inoculation. Since
the ﬁrst respiratory tissues were taken 3 days post-inoculation, it could be speculated whether the
timing of sampling was too late for analyzing the innate immune response. The innate immune
response engages within hours post-infection (Chumakov et al. 2021), which is why sampling one
day post-inoculation or earlier could have been more appropriate. In addition, sampling on day 2
- 5 would also be of interest in order to follow gene regulation development. Many of the selected
antiviral host genes did not show regulation throughout the respiratory tract in the huH3N2-infected
pigs, which could be attributed to several factors. Firstly, H3N2 strains have been described as an
upper respiratory disease in general (Richard et al. 2020), which may explain the lack of response
in the lower respiratory tract. Secondly, the regulation occurred primarily in the nasal mucosal
tissue, which may indicate that the virus had been cleared in the nasal mucosa and therefore did not
spread further down the respiratory tract. Thirdly, the huH3N2 might be slower than the swH3N2
in infecting the epithelium of the URT in pigs. The huH3N2 strain has not adapted to pigs as host
in contrast to the swH3N2 and might show a different receptor afﬁnity (Ito et al. 1998), therefore
the response might not yet have been fully elicited.
The difference in host response upon viral infection with the human or swine-adapted strain
could be due to differences in HA glycosylation of the two strains used in the present study. It
has previously been shown that nucleotide similarity in the viral HA surface protein affects host
response (Yu et al. 2021). A study conducted in mice showed that glycosylation of the head region
of the HA protein has a major impact on the antigenicity of H3N2 strains (York, Stevens, and
Alymova 2019). The study showed that increasing glycosylation of the HA head region correlates
with decreased antigenicity. The study compared the number of antigenic shift events between
a human H1N1 IAV strain and human H3N2 IAV strain, where it was observed that the H3N2
strain had a strong trend of an increasing number of glycans since it was introduced to the human
population (York, Stevens, and Alymova 2019). This indicates that the H3N2 strains probably
will show less virulence than the H1N1 strains because it undergoes several antigenic shifts that
decreases the virulence (York, Stevens, and Alymova 2019). Further, Qiu, Van der Meulen, and
62
==PAGE:71==
6
DISCUSSION
Van Reeth demonstrate slower antigenic shifts in the HA protein in H3N2 IAV in pigs compared to
humans, and therefore, human-adapted H3N2 are becoming more divergent from the swine-adapted
H3N2 IAV strain (Qiu, Van der Meulen, and Van Reeth 2013). The two studies collectively provide
information that might explain why the responses seen in this thesis are different between the two
strains. In the present study, we speculated that the huH3N2 strain had undergone more antigenic
shifts in the HA protein in order to adjust to the human population immunity (Qiu, Van der Meulen,
and Van Reeth 2013), which results in less nucleotide similarity and therefore a less active response
in pigs. Thus, it could be interesting to investigate similarities and differences of the HA protein
between the two strains to explain the different host responses seen in the present study.
Interferon Stimulated Genes are Central Against IAV Infection
Almost all selected ISGs were found to be regulated upon infection with swH3N2 IAV, of which
some have been selected for further discussion. The selection of ISGs was based on their role as
antiviral effectors at different stages of the viral life cycle (Schneider, Chevillotte, and Rice 2014).
In addition, a signiﬁcant up-regulation of the IRF7 (Figure 15) and IRF9 (Appendix 13 - qPCR
data, Intraplate) genes, necessary for ISG production and combating virus was found after infection
with swH3N2. Previously, it has been shown that deletion of these two genes makes the host more
susceptible to H1N1 Inﬂuenza A infection in mice (Hatesuer et al. 2017), and it is assumed that
this is also the case for the H3N2 as these genes are essential for the production of the antiviral ISGs.
One of the signiﬁcantly regulated ISGs is viperin (RSAD2), which has been shown to act as
an antiviral agent against many enveloped viruses by disrupting the budding of the virus from the
host cell, and has shown to have an effect against H1N1 inﬂuenza A virus in mice (Wang, Hinson,
and Cresswell 2007). In the current study, viperin (Figure 16) was signiﬁcantly up-regulated in the
swH3N2-infected pigs in all three tissues, suggesting that viperin is also essential for ﬁghting the
swH3N2 inﬂuenza A virus.
The OASL gene was also seen up-regulated in swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3. In a previous
study with human cells, it was observed that OASL enhances RIG-I activation and is able to activate
RIG-I in the absence of TRIM25. It is therefore reasonable to assume that a higher OASL expression
contributes to a stronger antiviral immune response against IAV, since RIG-I is crucial in the ﬁght
against IAV (Zhu et al. 2014). This aligns with another study in mice, where OASL, OAS2, and
RNASEL were shown to regulate the ’early phase’ of viral infection (Choi et al. 2015). These genes
63
==PAGE:72==
6
DISCUSSION
are also up-regulated in this study in swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 (Figure 16). These ISGs,
except RNASEL, were not up-regulated in the huH3N2-infected pigs in this study, again indicating
a difference in host response. The ’early phase’ prediction is, however, contradicting to other results
of this study, which otherwise indicate that day 3 was too late to observe the full extent of the innate
immune response.
A study by Dediego et al. showed that IFI44 negatively regulates IFN responses upon IAV
infection in human cells and results in repressed ISG expression. Many ISGs were regulated in
swH3N2-infected pigs on day 3 in the current study, despite IFI44 up-regulation, see Appendix 13
- qPCR data, Intraplate. Diego and coworkers state that cellular homeostasis is starting to occur
when IFI44 is up-regulated (Dediego et al. 2019). Therefore, day 3 post-inoculation might be too
late to observe the full extent of the innate immune response and thereby the expression of the ISGs
even though a high regulation was seen (Figure 16).
Lack of Cytokines and Chemokines Expression
Some of the cytokines released from innate immune cells were selected for this study to investigate
their regulation. Several cytokines and chemokines were down-regulated in the nasal mucosal
tissue. IL8, IL1β, and CXCL2 were down-regulated in both groups in the nasal mucosa, and
CCL2 and CCL3 were also down-regulated in the huH3N2-infected pigs in the nasal mucosa,
see Appendix 13 - qPCR data, Intraplate. These cytokines and chemokines are chemoattractants
for monocytes, macrophages, and neutrophils, among others (Hughes and Nibbs 2018; Brennan
and Zheng 2007; Parry et al. 1998). This might explain the limited lesions found in the nasal
mucosal histopathology analysis. Additionally, it might also explain that fewer lesions were found
in the huH3N2 compared to the swH3N2 pigs in the nasal mucosal. The down-regulation also
indicates that day 3 post-inoculation might be too late to observe the early response upon IAV
infection, and the cell homeostasis has begun (Hughes and Nibbs 2018). This is also emphasized
by several of the chosen cytokines and chemokines that did not show any signiﬁcant regulation in
the tracheal and lung tissue. Among the cytokines that did not show any signiﬁcant regulation in
the tracheal and lung tissue were IL6, IL8, IL1β, CCL2, and CCL3, which have been reported to be
up-regulated in human pulmonary microvascular endothelial cells infected with H1N1 Inﬂuenza A
virus (Kim et al. 2015). In addition, a study by Peiró et al. from 2018 showed a correlation between
the IL1β levels and the number of neutrophils in samples obtained from bronchoalveolar lavage
ﬂuid from mice infected with an H3N2 strain and that the number of neutrophils peaked 24 hours
64
==PAGE:73==
6
DISCUSSION
post-infection, which would equal the time for IL1β peak (Peiró et al. 2018). Furthermore, a similar
study investigating bronchoalveolar lavage ﬂuid from mice infected with H1N1 inﬂuenza A virus
showed that the level of IL6 peaked after 7 days of infection with relatively low levels of IL6 on
day 3 (Dienz et al. 2012). This indicates that it might have been too late to measure any signiﬁcant
regulation of IL1β, and too early or too late for measurement of IL6 regulation in the current study.
The low levels of mRNA coding for cytokines in the tracheal and lung tissue also align with the
histopathological results, where few macrophages and neutrophils were observed. Additionally, the
low levels of cytokines and chemokines should not be due to a low viral load, according to a former
study investigating the pandemic 2009 H1N1 IAV strain, which has grouped the Cq values as: low
Cq, 20; intermediate Cq, >20–30; and high Cq, >30 (Lalueza et al. 2019). This indicates that most
of the samples in this thesis lie in the intermediate category, and thus, the viral load in the samples
should be high enough to elicit a cytokine response, however limited regulation is detected.
Although most cytokines and chemokines were down-regulated or showed no expressional
change, the chemokines CXCL9 and CCR7 were up-regulated in swH3N2-infected pigs in all
tissues, and CXCL10 was up-regulated in swH3N2-infected pigs in trachea and lung (see Appendix
13 - qPCR data, Intraplate). CXCL9, CXCL10, and CCR7 are known for their stimulatory effect
on cell migration, differentiation, and activation, among others (Hughes and Nibbs 2018). These
chemokines can also attract T cells to the site, which is part of the adaptive immune response.
Thus, the innate and adaptive immune responses may overlap (Hughes and Nibbs 2018). The
chemokines that are seen up-regulated in this study are primarily involved in both the innate and
adaptive immune response. This could indicate that the transaction towards the adaptive immune
defense has begun. However, adaptive immune response to infection should be investigated in
further studies to conﬁrm this, which will be discussed in the section Future Perspectives.
The Limitations of the Histopathological Analysis
Histopathological examination was performed on different tissues from the respiratory system,
namely the nasal mucosa, trachea, and lungs, after infection with two different strains of IAV H3N2.
Tissues from the swH3N2-group had the most severe lesions, and the lesions were signiﬁcantly
different from the control group in the nasal mucosal and lung tissue (p = 0.01 and p = 0.05,
respectively), and from the huH3N2-group in the lung tissue (p = 0.01). However, the results of
this study are difﬁcult to compare with earlier published studies since this study compared different
origins within the same virus subtype, and other studies either compare different virus subtypes or
65
==PAGE:74==
6
DISCUSSION
different virus strains originated from the same host.
Tissue from the nasal mucosal, tracheal, and bronchi contain the same type of respiratory
epithelial cells (G.
Crystal et al. 2008), and therefore the background staining of the MAC
staining was presumed to be present in all of these tissues; however, it was only observed in
the nasal mucosal and tracheal epithelium. The background staining could be attributed to the
binding of MAC387 to calprotectin. Staphylococcus aureus (S. aureus) is a common bacterium
found in livestock pigs, especially in the nasal cavity, where it is found to a greater extent in the
URT and upper part of LRT than the lower part of the LRT and is typically not present in the
lungs except in cases with bacterial pneumonia (Krismer et al. 2017).
This is consistent with
the ﬁndings of this study, where most background staining occurred in the nasal mucosal epithelium.
One of the limitations of the present study was the time point at which the tissue was examined.
Lyoo et al. found in a macroscopic lung study that lesions in H3N2-infected pigs decrease day
2 post-inoculation (Lyoo et al. 2014).
Even though the study only performed a macroscopic
study, it might also affect the tissues microscopically.
In the current study, the tissues were
examined at day 3 post-inoculation, and therefore the worst histopathological outcome may not
have been captured. However, viral quantiﬁcation of nasal swabs (performed by the University
of Copenhagen) suggests that the highest viral load was present at 3 days post-inoculation for the
swH3N2-infected pigs (data not shown). In contrast, the huH3N2-infected pigs had the highest
viral load 2 days post-inoculation (data not shown). This might also explain why the lung tissue of
the swH3N2-infected pigs from day 3 post-inoculation has a higher histopathological score than
the huH3N2-infected pigs. However, it is important to mention that the viral load is measured from
nasal swabs, and a different outcome may be present if viral RNA quantiﬁcation was performed
on the respiratory tissues (Esteva-Socias et al. 2020; Erickson et al. 2009). In agreement with the
gene expression analysis, it could also be interesting to perform a histopathological analysis in
sequential days to clarify when the lesions are most severe for the two origins and if the huH3N2
lesions are worse than the swH3N2 lesions at any time point.
Another limitation of the study was the possible effect of ﬁxating tissue, as it was difﬁcult
to differentiate between healthy collapsed tissue and affected inﬁltrated alveolar tissue when
evaluating the alveolar interstitium.
Formalin was injected into the lungs by semi perfusion
to inﬂate the lung tissues selected for histopathology, which succeeded in some of the tissues,
66
==PAGE:75==
6
DISCUSSION
however, intratracheal perfusion would have been the most optimal method (Davenport et al. 2020).
The intratracheal perfusion method was not applied in the present study, since it would limit the
opportunity to perform additional analysis on the tissues. The difﬁculty in differentiating between
healthy and affected tissues is reﬂected in observations from control pigs, where most tissues were
scored as ’moderate’ (25% affected), however, the swH3N2-infected and huH3N2-infected
pigs were generally scored higher. The ﬁnding of lesions in the control pigs could also be due
to the presence of other pathogens than IAV, for example, pathogens associated with porcine
respiratory disease complex, which are able to induce histopathological changes even though no
gross lesions are visible (Hansen et al. 2010). The presence of red blood cells was considered when
differentiating between the healthy collapsed tissue and affected inﬁltrated alveolar tissue, as red
blood cells suggest healthy collapsed tissue, however, it might still be inadequate and should be
taken into consideration.
The extent of the histopathological analysis (n = 12) should also be taken into consideration,
as it cannot be ruled out that a different histopathological picture would have been obtained if
all pigs (n = 30) were included in the study. It was not possible to include all pigs due to time
limitation of the study. Furthermore, the scoring schemes used in this study are not standardized,
which can be problematic for the reproducibility of the study. However, to validate the results,
random samples were blinded and re-evaluated. Additionally, a third party was given the scoring
schemes and evaluated random samples. All samples were evaluated twice, and the same score was
given in all evaluations.
It is essential to note that the total histopathological score (Figure 31) is based on H&E and
MAC staining, and not IAV staining, since, as mentioned above, inﬂammation may have been
present without the presence of IAV as the pigs are livestock. However, a high amount of IAV
without severe inﬂammation and cell inﬁltration is also possible, since some IAV strains avoid
triggering the host immune response due to adaptation (Schmolke and García-Sastre 2010). No
lesions in trachea were found in the current study. This aligns with a study where H1N1 pdm09
infected pigs also showed no lesions except 1/29 pigs which had mild lesions in trachea (Schwaiger
et al. 2019). It is important to note that even though no lesions were found in the tracheal tissue,
IAV-positive cells were found.
Even though IAV-positive cells were not found in every tissue in the histopathological analysis,
67
==PAGE:76==
6
DISCUSSION
virus was present.
RNA from Inﬂuenza measured by qPCR (performed by the University of
Copenhagen) has a higher sensitivity than the IAV staining. When comparing qPCR results of
the viral load and the histopathological scores of the nasal mucosal and lung tissue, a correlation
was seen (see 9 and 10 in Appendix 17 - Correlation between viral RNA quantiﬁcation and total
histopathological score of H&E and MAC staining for the nasal mucosal and lung tissue.). This
shows that a higher viral load equals a higher histopathological score.
68
==PAGE:77==
7
CONCLUSION
7
Conclusion
The emergence of zoonotic IAV poses a severe risk to global health, and it is therefore, essential
to get a better understanding of the impact on the host and the zoonotic potential of IAV. Pigs are
considered a great mixing vessel for new reassortant viruses from both human and swine IAV, and
moreover, intercontinental pig transport has contributed to the worldwide spread of IAV strains.
This gives the virus a great opportunity to mix between strains, and therefore, surveillance has
signiﬁcant importance.
The primary aim was to understand the antiviral host response upon swH3N2 and huH3N2
infection in pigs through qPCR and histopathology. The results conducted from the current study
can be used to evaluate host responses after infection with swH3N2 and huH3N2.
The ﬁrst
hypothesis was conﬁrmed as the results showed that the swH3N2 IAV elicits a higher antiviral innate
immune response in infected pigs than that observed with huH3N2 IAV. Especially, the expression
of several ISGs seems to have great importance against IAV as many showed up-regulation after
swH3N2 IAV infection.
The second hypothesis was conﬁrmed as the qPCR results showed that most genes were
regulated in the nasal mucosal tissue upon infection with both the swH3N2 and huH3N2 IAV
strains. However, the swH3N2 IAV strain also affects the expression of genes in tracheal and
lung tissue, whereas the huH3N2 strain does not elicit any noticeable gene regulation response
in these respiratory tissues. Additionally, the clustering of the PCA plots and table 3 conﬁrmed a
difference in gene expression between tissues, conﬁrming the hypothesis that there is a different
immune response in different tissues upon an IAV infection.
The
third
hypothesis
was
also
conﬁrmed
as
the
histopathology
showed
that
the
swH3N2-infected pigs had more lesions in all investigated tissues as compared to huH3N2-infected
pigs. This indicates that the swH3N2 IAV elicited a more severe response than huH3N2 IAV.
However, it is important to note that only a few IAV-positive cells were observed, there was limited
cell inﬁltration, and in general normal tissue was observed. Even though there was limited sign of
affected tissue, there was a positive correlation between the total histopathological score and viral
RNA measured by qPCR, meaning a higher viral load equals a higher histopathological score.
In conclusion, the studies conducted through this master thesis provides excellent insight into
the different host response upon infection of different origins of IAV, which can be useful for future
seasonal vaccines and novel antiviral therapeutics
69
==PAGE:78==
8
FUTURE PERSPECTIVES
8
Future Perspectives
In the future, it would be interesting to analyze the level of expression of viral genes compared to
the innate immune response-related genes. The viral fold change in regulation is not touched upon
in this thesis since the viral genes did not get ampliﬁed with designed primers. To discuss the fold
change of each host gene, viral new primers should be designed, and the amount of viral RNA
should be measured to observe if the two-fold changes show any correlation. Further measurement
of viral RNA could have contributed to the discussion about immune evasion, which is a known
ability of inﬂuenza A virus. The viral genes NS, PB1-F2, and PA-X are known to modulate the
host immune response (Klemm et al. 2018), and therefore the experimental evidence that these
were present in the samples could drive the discussion about the gene regulation of host genes in
the inﬂuence of viral immune escape.
It could also be interesting to do proteomics on the same antiviral innate host genes used for
qPCR. mRNA and protein abundance has a weak correlation (Maier, Güell, and Serrano 2009),
meaning that not all mRNA is translated into proteins. Using proteomics shows which proteins
are present during an inﬂuenza A infection, which will be more reliable than only measuring
the mRNA. Techniques that could be used for this are global bottom-up proteomics with
Data-independent acquisition (DIA) mass spectrometry strategy.The proteomic analysis measuring
the relative protein abundance can be compared with the viral load, which gives a more accurate
picture of the viral burden on the host immune response.
The tissue used for this experiment could also be used to investigate the adaptive immune
defense, since samples from day 14 have been collected. In this matter, genes related to the adaptive
immune defense should be included in the qPCR analysis instead. Examples of genes that could be
interesting to look at are the genes encoding for the major histocompatibility complex (MHC) I and
II. They present peptides from pathogens to T cells, and therefore possess an important part of the
adaptive immune response (Janeway 2001). However, the MHC complexes are encoded by more
than 200 genes in humans (Janeway 2001), meaning that a more thorough investigation for gene
selection must be carried out.
Another aspect that could be interesting to look at is the immunoglobulin response produced
by the B cells, as antibodies can be associated with different states of disease (Ling et al. 2020).
70
==PAGE:79==
8
FUTURE PERSPECTIVES
Immunoglobulin (Ig) A, IgG, and IgM are crucial upon an IAV infection, as they protect the
mucosal respiratory epithelium against IAV, protect the lower respiratory tract against IAV,
and neutralize IAV through complement activation respectively (J.
H.C.M. Kreijtz, Fouchier,
and G.
F. Rimmelzwaan 2011).
Following the histopathology road, it is also possible to do
immunohistochemical staining of, for example, immunoglobulins, subsets of T cells, B cells, and
MHC complexes to visualize the activity of the adaptive immune system. This can be done by
using monoclonal antibodies that target a single epitope speciﬁc for each target (Whiteland et al.
1995). Hence, much information can still be retrieved from the samples from day 14 that has not
yet been investigated.
There was a limited amount of macrophages and neutrophils present in the histopathology.
It could therefore be interesting to also analyze TGFβ in future studies, since it is known for
inhibiting macrophages. In a mice study, it was shown that there was an increase of TGFβ within 1
day of inﬂuenza H5N9 infection. Their study indicated that TGFβ was activated by the NA protein
of IAV. Therefore, TGFβ might not be activated for the strain used in this study, however, it could
be interesting to investigate (Schultz-Cherry and Hinshaw n.d.; Zhang and An 2007).
Additionally, low transcriptional levels of cytokines and chemokines were observed in the qPCR
data, which are chemoattractants for macrophages and neutrophils, among others (Hughes and
Nibbs 2018). It could therefore be interesting to stain for cytokine and/or chemokine antibodies,
for example, an IL-6 antibody, to observe if there is also a limited amount of the IL-6 protein
present in the tissue (K Suker et al. 2017).

9
APPENDIX
Appendix 2 - RNA Puriﬁcation Protocol
Protokol: Puriﬁcation of miRNA and mRNA from tissue (RNA later) using GentleMACS
Kit: miRNeasy Mini kit (Qiagen, #217004) and RNase-Free DNase set (Qiagen, #79254)
Homonogenizer: GentleMACS + M-tubes + samples in RNAlater at -20 degrees
1. Place the centrifuge at 4◦C (Ole Dich)
2. Wash the working area with 0.5% NaOH solution and ﬁnd homogenizer, scalpel, weighing
trays, tweezers, pipettes, tips and pipettes.
3. Dilute RPE/RWT buffer with 100% ethanol at ﬁrst time use.
4. Samples are collected from -20 degrees freezer.
5. Transfer 1 ml of QIAzol Lysis Reagent to the desired number of M-tubes.
6. GentleMACS can homogenize 2 tubes at a time. Remove max 30 mg tissue with scalpel (a
small piece of about 3x3mm), cut into smaller pieces and transfer to M-tube with lysis buffer.
It is important that the tissue is placed on the edge of the cut as there is so little material that
it can easily end up at the top of the pipe when the machine starts. After transfer, the tissue is
homogenized on program RNA_02.01 (fresh tissue). Tubes with homogenate remain at room
temperature (15-25°C) for 5 minutes - or until the other samples are ﬁnished. Tweezers are
cleaned with NaOH and possibly also the waste is wiped with NaOH.
7. Add 200 µl of chloroform, close the lid of the tube and shake vigorously for 15 seconds. The
tube is set at room temperature for 2-3 min.
8. Transfer homogenate mix to a 1,5 ml tube (found in kit) and centrifuge at 12000 g/14000 rpm
for 15 min at 4°C. Switch off for cooling function.
9. The homogenate is now divided into 3 phases. From the top phase is taken approx. 300µl for a
2ml tube (found in kit). 450 µl (1:1,5) of 100% ethanol is added in the tube. The homogenate
is mixed with the pipette and the pipette tip is left in the tube.
10. Pipette up to 600µl of sample into a RNeasy Mini Spin Column in a 1,5ml collection tube
(found in kit). Close the lid and centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 15 sec at 20-25°C.
Discard the ﬂow-through.
85
==PAGE:94==
9
APPENDIX
11. Remove DNase and RDD buffer from the freezer.
12. Repeat step 10 with the rest of the samples (up to 600µl). Discard again the ﬂow-through.
13. Pipette 350 µl RWT buffer (check that buffer solute is not precipitated) to the column and
centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 15 sec at 20-25°C. Discard the ﬂow-through.
14. Mix DNase I incubation mix: add 10 µl DNase I stock solution to 70 µl Buffer RDD (found
in RNase-Free DNase set). Gently mix with the pipette.
15. Pipette 77 µl DNase I incubation mix directly onto the RNeasy silica gel membrane and place
the column at room temperature for 15 min (minimum time).
16. Pipette 350 µl RWT buffer to the column and centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 15 sec at
20-25°C. Discard the ﬂow-through.
17. Add 500 µl of RPE buffer to the column and centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 15 sec at
20-25°C. Discard the ﬂow-through. (Washing step)
18. Add 500 µl of RPE buffer again to the column and centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 2 min
at 20-25°C. Discard the ﬂow-through.
19. Centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 1 min without adding any buffer. Now the column is
ethanol free, therefore make sure that the column does not touch the tube when removed.
20. To elute, transfer the column to a new 1,5 ml tube, then add 30 µl of RNase free water to the
RNeasy silica gel membrane. Centrifuge at 8000 g/10000 rpm for 1 min at 20-25°C.
21. Flow-through is reused and pipetted again on the membrane, after which the column is
centrifuged at 8000 g/ 10000 rpm for 2 min at 20-25°C.
22. Check the concentrations and purity of the RNA extracted by NANO drop.
86

APPENDIX
Appendix 4 - Measurement of RNA Integrity Protocol
Protocol 1A: Measurement of RNA integrity
NB! Protocol 1 will be carried out by Karin and Betina as loading and priming of the RNA 6000
Nano chip require high skill level and experience.
Materials:
• Agilent RNA 6000 Nano Kit
• RNase-free water
• Micro centrifuge (≥1300 g)
• Heating block for ladder/sample denaturation
Always wear gloves when handling RNA and use RNase-free tips, tubes and water.
Prepared in advance
• Pipette 550 µl of RNA 6000 Nano gel matrix into a spin ﬁlter.
• Centrifuge at 1500 g ± 20 % for 10 minutes at room temperature.
• Aliquot 65 µl ﬁltered gel into 0.5 ml RNase-free microfuge tubes. Store at 4 ◦C.
Preparing the RNA ladder (after arrival of the kit)
• After reagent kit arrival pipette the Ladder in RNase-free vial
• Heat denature it for 2 min at 70 ◦C.
• Immediately cool down the vial on ice.
• Prepare aliquots in RNase-free vials with the required amount for a typical daily use.
• Store aliquots at -70◦C. After initial heat denaturing, the frozen aliquots needn’t be heat
denatured again.
PROTOCOL
1. Thaw the 10 RNA samples at 4◦C.
89
==PAGE:98==
9
APPENDIX
2. Ladder, Nano marker and nano-gel dye mix must equilibrate for 30 min to room temperature
before use.
Protect dye and dye mixtures from light.
Remove light covers only when
pipetting. The dye decomposes when exposed to light and this reduces the signal intensity.
3. Preparing the Gel-Dye Mix (use within 1 day)
(a) Vortex RNA 6000 Nano dye for 10 seconds, spin down and add 1 µl of dye into a 65 µl
aliquot of ﬁltered gel (see Preparing the Gel below). Quantity enough for 2 chips.
(b) Vortex solution well. Spin tube at 13000 g for 10 min at room temperature.
(c) Go to 7B place on ice.
4. Starting the Chip Run
(a) Start the computer and open the program: 2100 expert.
(b) Clean electrode, use the electrode cleaning chip. RNase-free water for 1 min. (pipette
350 µl RNase free water in a random well). Leave the bioanalyzer to dry for at least 10
sec. before use.
(c) Select Assays eukaryote total RNA Nano.
5. Loading the Gel-Dye Mix
(a) Place a RNA 6000 Nano chip on the chip priming station.
(b) Pipette 9.0 µl of gel-dye mix in the well marked in Figure 32A.
(c) Make sure that the plunger is positioned at 1 ml and then close the chip priming station.
(d) Press plunger until it is held by the clip.
(e) Wait for 30 seconds then release clip.
(f) Wait for 5 seconds slowly pull back plunger to 1ml position.
(g) Open chip priming station and pipette 9.0 µl of gel-dye mix in the wells marked in
Figure 32B.
6. Loading the Agilent RNA 6000 Nano Marker
(a) Pipette 5 µl of RNA 6000 Nano marker in all 13 sample wells marked in Figure 32C.
7. Loading the Ladder and Samples
90
==PAGE:99==
9
APPENDIX
(a) Pipette 1 µl of prepared ladder in well marked in Figure 32D.
(b) To minimize secondary structure, heat denature (70◦C, 2 minutes) the samples before
loading. Place on ice.
(c) Pipette 1 µl of sample (range 25-500 ng) in each of the 12 sample wells. Pipette 1 µl of
RNA 6000 Nano Marker in each unused sample well as marked in Figure 32E.
(d) Vortex the chip for 1 min at 2400 rpm.
(e) Run the chip in the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer within 5 min after loading the samples.
(f) Load the chip and press start, select data ﬁle and add sample name.
(g) After end run, clean the electrodes as described above.
Figure 32: Loading order of chip for the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer
Protocol 1B: Measurement of RNA amount (A260) and purity (A260/A230 and A260/A280)
NB! Use gloves (to protect your samples against RNases).
1. Select the appropriate type of biomolecule on the touch screen.
2. Follow the instructions on the touch screen to make a “blank” measurement using 1.3 µl
RNase-free water applied as a droplet on the pedestal. Lower the arm of the instrument – it
will automatically initiate measurement when the arm is lowered. NB! Make sure to have a
good grip on the arm when raising and lowering it.
3. Raise the sampling arm and wipe the pedestal and arm with ﬁlter paper.
4. Enter sample name (“team number”.”sample number”) on the touch screen.
5. Apply 1.3 µl of provided RNA samples and carefully close the arm to initiate measurement.
6. After measurement, raise the arm and clean the sample off the pedestal and arm with ﬁlter
paper.
91
==PAGE:100==
9
APPENDIX
7. Enter the next sample name on the touch screen and apply 1.3 µl of the next RNA sample.
Lower arm to measure, clean arm and pedestal with ﬁlter paper after measurement.
8. Repeat for all 10 samples. Finally, wipe arm and pedestal with a bit of RNase-free water on
ﬁlter paper, followed by dry ﬁlter paper.

Protokol: cDNA-synthesis, FLUIDIGM
Kit: QuantiTect Reverse Transcription Kit (Qiagen # 205311) + 1x Low EDTA TE-Buffer pH = 8.
1. Best results are achieved by adding 500 ng total RNA for cDNA synthesis

RT buffer at room temperature.
4. Mark micro centrifuge tubes with cDNA code
5. To each micro tube with cDNA code: Add 1.5 µl gDNA Wipeout Buffer (7x). Add RNase-free
water and RNA according to the table (total: µL).

APPENDIX
Appendix 7 - mRNA pre-ampliﬁcation Protocol
Protokol: mRNA preampliﬁcation and exonuclease treatment, FLUIDIGM
Materialer: Primer mix (200nM), cDNA, TaqMan PreAmp Master Mix (Applied Biosystems, PN
4391128), 1x Low EDTA TE-Buffer (pH=8), Exonuclease I (New england biolabs, PN MO293L)
1. Primer mix, (max. 200 pcs. single primer (100 pairs)) in our inner room (RNA/DNA free
room), shake all primers by hand, and spin down if necessary:
(a) First make a 20µM solution of each primer pair (10µl F primer (100µM) + 10 µl R
primer (100µM) + 30 µl low EDTA TE-buffer) NB! This solution will also be used to
assay Mix. (qPCR)
(b) Primer mix: 5 µl 20µM of the desired primer solution are mixed and added low EDTA
TE-buffer to 500 µl (200 nM solution). Aliqout primer mix in 3-4 tubes with name and
date.
2. Thaw frozen cDNA samples by placing them in a refrigerator.
3. PreAmp mix is made in the inner room, mini-eppendorf tubes are marked with preAmp code.
x1
x240
TagMan PreAmp Master Mix
3 µL
720 µL
1x Low TE buffer (pH=8)
2 µL
480 µL
200 nM primer mix (ryst og spin ned)
2,5 µL
600 µL
4. Add 7.6 µl PreAmp mix to the marked mini eppendorf tubes in the inner room. Take the tubes
to lab and add 2.5µl cDNA (50-100ng), mix with pipette.
5. Incubate on Thermocycler, program PREAMP : 95◦C , 10 min ! 95◦C, 15 sec ! 60◦C, 4
min, goto 2. 14 x ! 4◦C i 99 timer. 19 cycles total. NB! choose the program with the right
number of cycles!!!
6. Dilute the exonuclease to 4U/µl (3,5µl MiliQ, 0,5µl reaction buffer, 1µl Exo 20U)/cDNA.
Don’t make ekstra.
7. Add 4 µl Exo 4U to the 10 µl preAmp and mix.

hours.

exonucleas treated cDNA is added to 18 µL low EDTA TE buffer, and are stored at -20◦C.
10. Preampliﬁed and exonuclease treated cDNA to be used for dilution series can be frozen
undiluted..

Appendix 8 - qPCR Protocol
Protocol: EvaGreen, Dynamic array 96.96
Materials: Primer mix (20µM), TaqMan Gene Expression Master mix (Applied Biosystems, PN
4369016), EvaGreen 20X (VWR, BTIU31000), Low EDTA TE-Buffer (VWR, APLIA8569.0500),
BMK-M-96.96 (Fluidigm), 85000800 sample and assay loading kit (Fluidigm), 100-0388 DNA
binding Dye (Fluidigm)
Turn on the HX IFC controller, let the 20X DNA Binding Dye thaw in the refrigerator in the inner
room.
1. Wipe the table with 10% microsol.
2. Dispense 3 µL 2X Assay loading Reagent assay mastermix in a microtiter plate, then add 3
µL 20µM of speciﬁc primer pair. Put sealing tape on the plate and put it in the refrigerator.
3. Make Pre-sample mix:

Add 4.6 µL pre-sample mix to each well in a new microtiter plate.
4. In lab: Vortex and add 1.5 µL preampliﬁed cDNA to the plate with 4.6 µL Pre-sample-mix,
and put the plate in the refrigerator.
5. Add ”control line ﬂuid” to the chip, check that the valves are moving with syringe, the liquid
is injected while pushing the valve, the liquid will run relatively easily through.
6. Remove the protective ﬁlm from the chip (save it) and prepare this in HX IFC controller (takes
20 minutes). Choose: Chip Prime 136x, User, run script. The chip should be used within one
hour after priming.
7. Place the chip on a dark surface, with the "cut" corner at the upper left. Vortex assay mix and
spin down* add (5 µL) in the left side of the chip. Vortex sample mix and spin down* add (5

µL) in the right side of the chip. * Centrifuge for 60 sec at room temperature and 1000 rpm.

8. Put the chip back in the HX IFC controller and run the Load Mix (136x) program. This takes
90 min. Transfer the chip to the BioMark machine shortly after the end of loading in the
controller.
9. Turn on the BioMark at least ½ hour before the chip is ﬁnish in the HX controller (the green

window.
